2:25:1619:0:14:72:336:700:12:Times:12:Times:C:A Portrait of Trade in Value Added over Four Decades
2:26:1671:14:14:72:278:686:12:Times:12:Times:C:Robert C. Johnson and Guillermo Noguera
2:27:1710:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:C:NBER Working Paper No. 22974
2:28:1738:14:14:72:148:658:12:Times:12:Times:C:December 2016
2:29:1751:14:28:72:159:644:12:Times:12:Times:C:JEL No. F1,F4,F6
2:30:1767:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:A:ABSTRACT
2:31:1775:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:C:We  combine  data  on  trade,  production,  and  input  use  to  document  changes  in  the  value  added 
2:32:1881:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:C:content of  trade  between  1970  and  2009.    The  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports  fell  by 
2:33:1986:14:14:71:545:560:12:Times:12:Times:C:roughly   10   percentage points   worldwide.      The   ratio   declined   20   percentage   points   in 
2:34:2091:14:14:71:545:546:12:Times:12:Times:C:manufacturing, but rose in non-manufacturing sectors.  Declines also differ across countries and 
2:35:2187:14:14:72:545:532:12:Times:12:Times:C:trade  partners:  they  are  larger  for  fast  growing  countries, for  nearby  trade  partners,  and  among 
2:36:2296:14:14:72:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:C:partners that adopt regional trade agreements.  Using a multi-sector structural gravity model with 
2:37:2394:14:14:72:546:504:12:Times:12:Times:C:input-output linkages, we show that changes in trade frictions play a dominant role in explaining 
2:38:2491:14:42:72:140:490:12:Times:12:Times:C:all these facts.
2:39:2507:42:14:72:161:448:12:Times:12:Times:C:Robert C. Johnson
2:40:2524:14:14:72:198:434:12:Times:12:Times:C:Department of Economics
2:41:2547:14:14:72:164:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:Dartmouth College
2:42:2564:14:14:72:179:406:12:Times:12:Times:C:6106 Rockefeller Hall
2:43:2585:14:14:72:170:392:12:Times:12:Times:C:Hanover, NH 03755
2:44:2602:14:14:72:124:378:12:Times:12:Times:C:and NBER
2:45:2610:14:28:72:231:364:12:Times:12:Times:C:robert.c.johnson@dartmouth.edu
2:46:2640:28:14:72:166:336:12:Times:12:Times:C:Guillermo Noguera
2:47:2657:14:14:72:198:322:12:Times:12:Times:C:Department of Economics
2:48:2680:14:14:72:183:308:12:Times:12:Times:C:University of Warwick
2:49:2701:14:14:72:257:294:12:Times:12:Times:C:Coventry, CV4 7AL, United Kingdom
2:50:2734:14:0:72:201:280:12:Times:12:Times:C:g.noguera@warwick.ac.uk
3:51:2757:0:18:89:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Recent decades have seen the emergence of global supply chains. Echoing Feenstra (1998),
3:52:2845:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:rising  trade  integration  has  coincided  with  the  simultaneous  disintegration  of  production
3:53:2944:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:across  borders.1As  inputs  pass  through  these  global  supply  chains,  they  typically  cross
3:54:3042:18:18:71:539:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:borders multiple times. Since the national accounts record gross shipments across the border,
3:55:3135:18:18:71:539:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:not  the  locations  at  which  value  is  added  at  different  stages  of  the  production  process,
3:56:3237:18:18:71:539:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:conventional trade data obscure how value added – and the primary factors embodied therein
3:57:3327:18:18:71:539:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– is traded in the global economy.  This means that gross trade data alone are not sufficient to
3:58:3423:18:18:71:539:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:isolate the causes or interpret the consequences of the massive changes in the global economy
3:59:3516:18:18:71:539:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that have occurred in recent decades.  We need to pierce the veil of the gross flows to analyze
3:60:3611:18:18:71:278:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes in trade in value added directly.
3:61:3652:18:18:89:539:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:This paper computes and analyzes the value added content of trade over the last four
3:62:3736:18:18:71:539:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decades (1970-2009). In doing so, we make three contributions. First, we provide long horizon
3:63:3829:18:18:71:539:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:measures of value-added trade for a wide cross section of countries.  Second, we document
3:64:3918:18:17:71:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:five stylized facts about changes in value-added and gross trade at the world, country, and
3:65:4009:17:18:71:539:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bilateral level over time.  We show that the value added content of trade has declined for
3:66:4099:18:18:71:539:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  world  as  a  whole,  that  there  is  substantial  heterogeneity  in  declines  across  countries,
3:67:4203:18:18:71:539:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and  that  regional  trade  agreements  lower  value-added  relative  to  gross  trade.   Third,  we
3:68:4303:18:18:71:539:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:use a trade model with input-output linkages across sectors and countries to quantify the
3:69:4392:18:18:71:539:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:role  of  international  trade  frictions  in  explaining  the  divergence  between  value-added  and
3:70:4493:18:18:71:539:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gross  trade  over  time.   We  show  that  changes  in  trade  frictions,  particularly  frictions  for
3:71:4597:18:18:71:440:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufactured inputs, play a key role in explaining all five stylized facts.
3:72:4672:18:18:89:539:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To  track  value-added  trade  over  time,  we  combine  time  series  data  on  trade,  produc-
3:73:4768:18:18:71:539:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tion, and input use to construct an annual sequence of global bilateral input-output tables
3:74:4859:18:18:71:539:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:covering forty-two countries back to 1970.  These synthetic tables track shipments of final
3:75:4950:18:18:71:539:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and intermediate goods both within and between countries.  Using this framework, we com-
3:76:5038:18:18:71:539:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:pute value-added exports:  the amount of value added from a given source country that is
3:77:5126:18:17:71:539:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:consumed in each destination (i.e.,  embodied in final goods absorbed in that destination)
3:78:5216:17:18:71:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:[Johnson  and  Noguera  (2012a)].   Value-added  exports  measure  international  transactions
3:79:5310:18:18:71:539:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in a manner consistent with commonly used value-added representations of production and
3:80:5397:18:18:71:540:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:preferences.2They differ from gross exports for several distinct reasons: exports are typically
3:81:5492:18:18:71:539:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:produced using imported inputs, some exported inputs return home embodied in imports,
3:82:5577:18:18:71:539:152:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and exported inputs often are processed in third countries before being shipped onto their
3:83:5667:18:16:85:539:134:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:1This theme is reflected in work on vertical specialization, offshoring, and global value chains [Feenstra
3:84:5773:16:8:72:471:118:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Hanson (1999), Yi (2003, 2010), Grossman and Rossi-Hansberg (2008), Antr`as (2016)].
3:85:5861:8:16:85:539:110:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:2On the production side, value-added exports are explicitly comparable to GDP. On the demand side,
3:86:5959:16:11:72:540:94:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:value-added imports equal final expenditure on value added from foreign sources, regardless of whether that
3:87:6066:11:0:72:340:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:value-added is embodied in domestic or imported final goods.
4:88:6127:0:18:72:158:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:final destination.
4:89:6145:18:18:89:539:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:This  data  work  builds  on  and  extends  an  active  literature  on  global  input-output  ac-
4:90:6242:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:counting and trade in value added.3Our data construction effort is distinguished from this
4:91:6332:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:related  work  in  that  we  provide  a  long  historical  perspective  on  the  rise  of  global  supply
4:92:6437:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:chains, with broad country scope.  In this, our work extends the pioneering long run analysis
4:93:6530:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of vertical specialization by Hummels, Ishii and Yi (2001), who measured the import content
4:94:6621:18:18:72:539:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of exports for ten OECD countries from 1970 to 1990.4The data that we compile span a
4:95:6705:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:period  of  major  structural  changes  in  the  global  economy,  including  the  rise  of  emerging
4:96:6806:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:markets, the (re-)integration of Europe, and the spread of regional trade agreements.  The
4:97:6896:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:long panel dimension of our data is essential to credibly identify the impact of these events.
4:98:6990:18:18:89:539:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We  summarize  the  most  significant  changes  in  value-added  versus  gross  trade  via  five
4:99:7086:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:stylized facts.  The first fact is that the ratio of value-added to gross exports is declining over
4:100:7185:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:time, by about ten percentage points over four decades [Fact 1].  Consistent with anecdotal
4:101:7276:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:evidence, this decline has accelerated over time: the ratio of value-added to gross exports has
4:102:7371:17:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fallen roughly three times as fast since 1990 as it did from 1970 to 1990.  This global decline
4:103:7466:18:18:72:540:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:masks  significant  heterogeneity  across  sectors,  countries,  and  bilateral  partners.   Across
4:104:7565:18:18:72:540:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sectors,  the  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports  has  fallen  by  almost  twenty  percentage
4:105:7667:18:18:72:540:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:points within manufacturing, but has risen outside manufacturing [Fact 2].  Across countries,
4:106:7760:18:18:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:declines in value-added to gross exports range from near zero to over twenty-five percentage
4:107:7852:18:18:72:479:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:points, and fast growing countries have seen larger declines on average [Fact 3].
4:108:7933:18:18:89:539:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Across bilateral partners, we show that both bilateral distance and adoption of bilateral
4:109:8022:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade agreements predict changes in value-added to gross export ratios.  In the time series,
4:110:8114:18:18:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distance is negatively correlated with changes in the bilateral ratio of value-added to gross
4:111:8207:18:18:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exports, so that the largest declines in value-added to export ratios are concentrated among
4:112:8299:18:18:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proximate trading partners [Fact 4].  We also find that adoption of regional trade agreements
4:113:8392:18:18:72:540:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(RTAs) is associated with declines in the ratio of bilateral value-added to gross exports [Fact
4:114:8487:18:17:72:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:5].  For a typical agreement, the ratio of value-added to gross trade falls by five to eleven
4:115:8580:17:18:72:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:percent.   Further,  deep  trade  agreements  (e.g.,  customs  unions  or  common  markets)  are
4:116:8676:18:18:72:526:206:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:associated with larger declines in value-added to export ratios than shallow agreements.5
4:117:8765:18:18:89:539:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To isolate the driving forces underlying these changes, we interpret the five facts through
4:118:8856:18:18:72:540:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the lens of the workhorse structural gravity model, augmented to include input-output link-
4:119:8947:18:16:85:539:152:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:3See Daudin, Rifflart and Schweisguth (2011), Johnson and Noguera (2012a), Johnson (2014), Timmer
4:120:9044:16:8:71:539:136:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:et al. (2014), Koopman, Wang and Wei (2014), Los, Timmer and de Vries (2015), and Kee and Tang (2016).
4:121:9146:8:16:85:539:128:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:4It also complements related data sets, such as the World Input-Output Database, which cover the post-
4:122:9248:16:8:71:146:112:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:1995 period only.
4:123:9265:8:16:85:539:104:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:5These findings contribute to an important literature on the impact of RTAs [Freund and Ornelas (2010)].
4:124:9369:16:11:71:539:88:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Despite the fact that many agreements were explicitly adopted to promote integration of supply chains across
4:125:9477:11:0:71:392:77:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:borders, existing evidence on whether or how they have done so is scarce.
5:126:9551:0:18:72:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ages across sectors and countries.6There are two steps in this analysis.  First, we use our
5:127:9642:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:global  input-output  data,  together  with  auxiliary  data  on  prices  of  real  value  added  and
5:128:9743:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:final  expenditure,  to  measure  changes  international  trade  frictions  and  sectoral  expendi-
5:129:9842:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ture weights for final goods and inputs.  Conditional on prices, the trade frictions influence
5:130:9936:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bilateral sourcing decisions for final goods and intermediate inputs, the final and input ex-
5:131:10029:18:18:72:513:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:penditure weights govern the allocation of final and inputs expenditure across sectors.
5:132:10116:18:18:89:539:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, we analyze counterfactual model simulations to evaluate the role of trade frictions
5:133:10207:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in explaining changes in value-added versus gross trade.7We show that declines in trade
5:134:10294:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions  over  time  play  an  important  role  in  explaining  all  five  stylized  facts  highlighted
5:135:10399:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:above.  Changes in other driving forces – including changes in productivity, factor supplies,
5:136:10492:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and sectoral expenditure weights – play a comparatively minor role.  Among trade frictions,
5:137:10583:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:declines  in  frictions  for  manufacturing  goods,  particularly  manufactured  inputs,  are  most
5:138:10682:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:important  for  matching  the  data.   We  also  show  that  declines  in  bilateral  trade  frictions
5:139:10784:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:associated  with  RTA  adoption  explain  changes  in  bilateral  value-added  to  export  ratios.
5:140:10882:17:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Moreover, the spread of RTAs over time can account for up to 15% of the global decline in
5:141:10971:18:18:72:252:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratio of value-added to gross trade.
5:142:11007:18:18:89:539:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The paper proceeds as follows.  Section 1 outlines the procedure and data we use to mea-
5:143:11095:18:18:72:540:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sure value-added exports.  Section 2 documents five stylized facts about differences between
5:144:11187:18:18:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gross and value-added exports.  Section 3 presents the model, our quantification procedures,
5:145:11279:18:18:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and results on the role of trade frictions versus other forces in accounting for the five facts.
5:146:11375:18:45:72:484:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We analyze the role of trade frictions in detail in Section 4.  Section 5 concludes.
5:147:11459:45:30:72:509:304:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:1    Measuring Value-Added Exports Through Time
5:148:11506:30:18:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  begin  by  laying  out  the  global  input-output  framework  and  procedure  for  computing
5:149:11602:18:18:72:540:256:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added exports, drawing on Johnson and Noguera (2012a).  We then briefly discuss how
5:150:11691:18:18:72:540:238:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we combine sector-level production, input use, and trade data to implement the calculations,
5:151:11783:18:18:72:271:220:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with details provided in the Appendix.
5:152:11821:18:15:85:539:202:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:6Our model shares many similarities with recent multi-sector Ricardian models, which incorporate trade
5:153:11923:15:12:72:540:187:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:in both final and intermediate inputs [Caliendo and Parro (2015), Levchenko and Zhang (2016), Eaton et al.
5:154:12029:12:12:72:540:175:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:(2016)].  Though we rely on Armington rather than Ricardian micro-foundations for trade,  the aggregate
5:155:12132:12:12:72:540:163:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:response of value-added trade to frictions would be similar in both models.  In contrast to recent Ricardian
5:156:12240:12:12:72:540:151:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:models, we allow differences in trade costs for final and intermediate goods to match imports of final and
5:157:12346:12:8:72:287:139:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:intermediate goods separately within each sector.
5:158:12395:8:16:85:539:131:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:7Our long horizon analysis complements Eaton et al. (2016), who examine high frequency (business cycle)
5:159:12498:16:12:72:540:115:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:variation in trade frictions.  Our results on the impact of regional trade agreements also complement Caliendo
5:160:12608:12:0:72:326:103:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Parro (2015), who study North American integration.
6:161:12664:0:26:72:346:708:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:1.1    Computing Value-Added Exports
6:162:12700:26:18:72:539:682:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Consider a world withNcountries andSsectors at datet. Output in each sector and country
6:163:12787:18:18:71:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is produced using domestic primary factors (capital, labor, etc.)  and intermediate inputs,
6:164:12878:18:18:71:539:646:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:which may be sourced from home or abroad.  Output is tradable in all sectors, and it may
6:165:12966:18:18:71:539:628:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:be used to satisfy final demand (consumption, investment, and government expenditure) or
6:166:13054:18:18:71:539:610:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:used as an intermediate input at home or abroad.  The market clearing condition for gross
6:167:13143:18:33:71:377:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:output produced by sectorsin countryican be written as:
6:168:13198:33:0:207:246:559:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:?:yit(s) =

6:171:13208:0:0:269:311:559:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:fijt(s) +
6:172:13217:0:25:314:331:571:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:A:∑
6:173:13218:25:0:320:323:546:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:A:j
6:174:13219:0:26:333:350:571:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:∑
6:175:13220:26:0:338:344:545:8:g_d0_f10:6:g_d0_f12:?:s′
6:176:13222:0:41:352:540:559:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:mijt(s,s′),(1)
6:177:13236:41:18:71:539:518:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereyit(s) is the value of output in sectorsof countryi,fijt(s) is the value of final goods

6:185:13900:33:33:260:540:360:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:A:yt= (I−At)−1ft,(2)

6:187:14002:18:0:72:243:309:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:ftis a block vector with elements

6:190:14061:0:23:396:540:314:8:g_d0_f13:12:g_d0_f2:A:−1is the Leontief Inverse of

6:192:14182:18:18:72:540:273:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:goods returns the value of output from each country and sector that is required to produce
6:193:14272:18:18:72:161:255:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:those final goods.

6:206:14858:25:15:85:539:109:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:8In  the  data  we  use,  we  observe  only  the  value  of  cross-border  transactions,  not  quantities  shipped.
6:207:14973:15:12:72:540:94:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Since markets implicitly clear in quantities, this means we are evaluating the underlying quantity flows at a
6:208:15082:12:0:72:540:82:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:common set of prices to ensure that revenue for producers equals the value of expenditure across destinations.

7:210:15284:18:39:72:407:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ofyijtdefined above.  We refer tovaijt(s) as value-added exports.
7:211:15349:39:26:72:143:651:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:1.2    Data
7:212:15360:26:18:72:540:625:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We combine data from the national accounts, commodity trade statistics, and benchmark
7:213:15445:18:18:72:540:607:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:input-output tables to construct global input-output tables for each year between 1970 and
7:214:15535:18:18:72:540:589:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:2009.  We include forty-two OECD countries and major emerging markets, which account
7:215:15619:18:18:72:540:571:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for around 90% of world GDP. Remaining countries are aggregated into a ‘rest of the world’
7:216:15709:18:18:72:539:553:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:composite.9We include four composite sectors: (1) agriculture, hunting, forestry, and fishing;
7:217:15803:18:18:72:491:535:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(2) non-manufacturing industrial production; (3) manufacturing; and (4) services.
7:218:15884:18:18:89:539:517:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Starting with macro-data on production and expenditure, we take annual GDP by com-
7:219:15966:18:17:72:540:499:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:posite  sector  and  GDP  by  expenditure  category  (i.e.,  final  expenditure,  exports,  and  im-
7:220:16066:17:18:72:540:482:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ports) from the UN National Accounts Database.  Using the share of goods and services in
7:221:16154:18:18:72:540:464:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exports/imports from the IMF Balance of Payments statistics, we split exports/imports in
7:222:16242:18:18:72:389:446:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the GDP data into goods (sectors (1)-(3) above) and services.
7:223:16303:18:18:89:539:428:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To measure bilateral trade, we use data from the NBER-UN and CEPII BACI Databases.
7:224:16385:18:18:72:540:410:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:For goods trade, we measure bilateral final and intermediate goods trade separately for each
7:225:16477:18:18:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of  the  three  goods  sectors.   To  do  this,  we  assign  disaggregated  commodity  codes  in  the
7:226:16578:18:18:72:540:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade data to either final or intermediate use, based on the mapping from commodities to
7:227:16666:18:18:72:540:356:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:national accounts end uses defined in the Broad Economic Categories (BEC) system.  We
7:228:16751:18:18:72:540:338:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:then aggregate commodities to match the composite sector definitions, using correspondences
7:229:16842:18:18:72:540:320:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:between commodity and industry classifications.  The result is a sector-level data set of cross-
7:230:16938:18:18:72:449:302:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:country final and intermediate goods shipments from 1970 to the present.
7:231:17010:18:18:89:539:284:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We turn to input-output tables for additional information on input use and sector-level fi-
7:232:17101:18:17:72:540:266:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:nal expenditure.  We take data for benchmark years from the OECD Input-Output Database
7:233:17187:17:18:72:540:249:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and IDE-JETRO Asian Input-Output Tables.  Input-output tables are available for all forty-
7:234:17277:18:18:72:540:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:two countries after 1995, for Asian countries from 1985, and ten major industrialized coun-
7:235:17368:18:18:72:470:213:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tries (the G7 plus Australia, Denmark, and the Netherlands) from the 1970’s.
7:236:17444:18:18:89:539:195:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In  combining  these  data,  we  face  two  challenges.   First,  there  are  discrepancies  across
7:237:17543:18:18:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:alternative data sources, both due to differences in definitions and measurement error.  Sec-
7:238:17636:18:18:72:540:159:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ond, whereas the national accounts and trade data are annual, the input-output tables are
7:239:17725:18:18:72:540:141:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:produced  for  benchmark  years  only,  which  are  asynchronous  across  countries.   To  resolve
7:240:17823:18:15:85:539:123:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:9The countries in our sample (listed in the Appendix) account for roughly 80% of world GDP and 70-80%
7:241:17924:15:12:72:540:108:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:of world trade in the 1970-1990 period, rising to over 90% of GDP and 80-90% of world trade after 1990.
7:242:18027:12:12:72:540:96:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Due to lack of data, we include the Czech Republic, Estonia, Russia, Slovakia, and Slovenia in the rest of
7:243:18133:12:0:72:227:84:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the world during 1970’s and 1980’s.
8:244:18169:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these issues, we prioritize the national accounts data and adjust the commodity trade data
8:245:18259:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in order to match the levels of aggregate trade in the national accounts.  We then use a con-
8:246:18352:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:strained least squares procedure to simultaneously adjust the input-output data to match
8:247:18440:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the annual production and trade data and extrapolate benchmark data to non-benchmark
8:248:18524:18:18:72:101:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:years.
8:249:18530:18:18:89:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The result is a sector-level data set containing gross output (yit), value-added to output
8:250:18620:18:18:72:539:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratios (rit(s)),  final demand for domestic and imported goods (fiitandfIit),  and domestic
8:251:18711:18:18:72:539:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and imported input use matrices (AiitandAIit) for forty-two countries.10For goods imports
8:252:18800:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(s={1,2,3}), we use observed bilateral final and intermediate import shares to splitfIit(s)
8:253:18891:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:andAIit(s,s′) across sources.  For the services sector, we apply a proportionality assumption;
8:254:18985:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we assume that bilateral import shares for final and intermediate services are the same and
8:255:19076:18:45:72:412:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:equal to the share of each partner in multilateral services imports.
8:256:19144:45:30:72:468:465:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:2    Changes in Value-Added vs. Gross Exports
8:257:19189:30:18:72:540:435:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Using the framework and data introduced above, we document five stylized time-series facts
8:258:19279:18:17:72:540:417:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:regarding  changes  the  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports  over  time.   We  start  at  the
8:259:19380:17:18:72:540:400:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:multilateral  level,  documenting  changes  at  the  world,  sector,  and  country  level.   We  then
8:260:19481:18:18:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:examine  how  proxies  for  bilateral  trade  frictions  have  shaped  changes  in  bilateral  value-
8:261:19582:18:18:72:540:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added versus gross exports.  These serve as focal points in the model accounting analysis
8:262:19671:18:39:72:200:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that follows in Section 3.
8:263:19697:39:26:72:371:307:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:2.1    World, Sector, and Country Changes
8:264:19738:26:18:72:540:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We start at the global level, plotting the ratio of value-added exports to gross exports for
8:265:19830:18:18:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  world  as  a  whole  in  Figure  1.   The  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports  declines  by
8:266:19934:18:18:72:539:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:0.08 including the ROW and 0.09 excluding the ROW from 1970-2009.  Excluding the 2009
8:267:20019:18:18:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade collapse, the value-added to export ratio declines by 0.11 including the ROW and by
8:268:20108:18:18:72:539:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:0.13 excluding the ROW.11This decline is spread unevenly over time.  During the 1970-1989
8:269:20197:18:17:72:540:191:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:interval, there is only a small net decline value added relative to gross exports, on the order
8:270:20292:17:16:81:539:174:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:10Because  we  do  not  have  production  or  input-output  data  for  countries  in  the  rest  of  the  world,  we
8:271:20408:16:12:72:540:158:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:assume that all exports from the forty-two countries in our data to the rest of the world are absorbed there.
8:272:20517:12:12:72:540:146:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:This assumption has little practical effect on measured value-added exports among countries in the sample
8:273:20622:12:8:72:210:134:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:[Johnson and Noguera (2012b)].
8:274:20652:8:16:81:539:126:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:11Bems, Johnson and Yi (2010) point to the composition of changes in final expenditure during the 2009
8:275:20754:16:12:72:540:110:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:recession to explain why value added rose relative to gross trade.  When spending on final goods from sectors
8:276:20863:12:12:72:540:98:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:with high degrees of vertical specialization (e.g., durable goods) falls, then the ratio of value-added to gross
8:277:20975:12:0:72:129:86:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:exports rises.
9:278:20990:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of a few percentage points.  In contrast, value added relative to gross exports fall rapidly, by
9:279:21086:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:8.5 (9.5 excluding the ROW) percentage points from 1990-2008.  The decline in the value-
9:280:21174:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added to export ratio is roughly three times as fast during the 1990-2008 period as during
9:281:21264:18:18:72:175:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the pre-1990 period.
9:282:21284:18:18:89:539:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To drill down, we disaggregate these global trends along two dimensions, into changes in
9:283:21372:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added to export ratios at the sector level and across countries.  We plot sector-level
9:284:21464:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes in Figure 2.  The headline result is that manufacturing is the only sector in which
9:285:21555:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the value-added to export ratio is falling over time.  The ratio is increasing for agriculture
9:286:21649:18:18:72:428:564:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:and services and stable in non-manufacturing industrial production.12
9:287:21718:18:18:89:539:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Figure 3, we plot changes in value-added to export ratios at the country level.  Panel
9:288:21807:18:18:71:539:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(a)  of  Figure  3  contains  cumulative  changes  in  the  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports
9:289:21909:18:18:71:539:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 1970-2008 at the country level.  Nearly all countries experienced declines in the ratio
9:290:22001:18:18:71:539:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of value-added to gross exports,  but the magnitude of the decline is heterogeneous across
9:291:22091:18:17:71:539:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries.   Most  experience  declines  larger  than  10  percentage  points,  though  some  large
9:292:22190:17:18:71:539:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and prominent countries (e.g.,  Japan,  the UK, and Brazil) have smaller declines.  Among
9:293:22279:18:18:71:539:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries  with  large  declines,  one  sees  many  emerging  markets,  but  also  some  important
9:294:22377:18:18:71:261:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:advanced economies (e.g., Germany).
9:295:22412:18:18:89:539:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To organize this cross-country variation, we plot the average annual change in the ratio
9:296:22500:18:18:71:539:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of value-added to gross exports against the average annual growth rate in real GDP in Panel
9:297:22591:18:18:71:539:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(b)  of  Figure  3.   The  correlation  is  negative  and  statistically  significant  at  the  1%  level.
9:298:22697:18:18:71:539:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Cumulated over four decades, the point estimate implies that a country at the 90thpercentile
9:299:22789:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the growth distribution (5.8% per year) has a decline in the ratio of roughly 0.21 while
9:300:22880:18:18:72:539:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a  country  at  the  10thpercentile  (2.2%  per  year)  has  a  decline  of  0.11.   Because  emerging
9:301:22982:18:18:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:markets  on  average  have  higher  growth  than  advanced  countries,  this  also  reinforces  the
9:302:23081:18:18:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:observation above that gross exports have risen more than value-added exports on average
9:303:23169:18:39:72:169:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for these countries.
9:304:23189:39:25:72:282:220:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:2.2    Bilateral Trade Frictions
9:305:23221:25:18:72:540:195:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Shifting  our  focus  to  bilateral  country  pairs,  we  describe  how  changes  in  bilateral  value-
9:306:23324:18:18:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added versus gross exports are shaped by bilateral trade frictions.  We focus on two common
9:307:23415:18:18:72:429:159:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proxies for bilateral frictions:  distance and regional trade agreements.
9:308:23488:18:16:81:539:141:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:12Note that the sector-level value-added to export ratio is not bounded by one.  The reason is that each
9:309:23592:16:11:72:540:125:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:sector can export value added directly,  embodied in gross exports from the sector,  or it can export value
9:310:23699:11:12:72:540:114:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:added indirectly, embodied in gross exports from other sectors.  For example, non-manufacturing inputs are
9:311:23805:12:12:72:540:102:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:used in production of manufactured goods, and hence non-manufacturing value added is indirectly exported
9:312:23909:12:0:72:190:90:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:embodied in manufactures.
10:313:23935:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:The Differential Burden of DistanceWe are interested in two main questions.  First,
10:314:24018:18:18:71:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:how do gross exports (xijt),  value-added exports (vaijt),  and value-added to export ratios
10:315:24110:18:18:72:539:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(V AXijt≡vaijtxijt) respond to bilateral distance?  Second, how have these responses changed
10:316:24202:18:18:72:125:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:over time?
10:317:24212:18:18:89:539:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To  answer  these  questions,  we  estimate  gravity-style  regressions  for  each  of  the  three
10:318:24310:18:33:72:175:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:variables of interest:
10:319:24332:33:33:200:540:585:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:log(yijt) =φyit+φyjt+βytlog(distij) +εijt,(3)
10:320:24377:33:18:72:539:552:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereyijt∈ {xijt,vaijt,V AXijt}),{φyit,φyjt}are  importer-year  and  exporter-year  fixed  ef-
10:321:24471:18:17:72:539:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fects  andβytis  the  time-varying  coefficient  on  bilateral  distance  (distij)  for  outcomey.
10:322:24569:17:18:72:539:517:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:For interpretation, it is useful to note thatβV AX=βva−βxholds by construction, since
10:323:24654:18:18:72:256:499:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:log(V AXijt) = log(vaijt)−log(xijt).
10:324:24690:18:18:89:539:481:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The distance coefficients estimated in Equation (3) are plotted in Figure 4.13Looking at
10:325:24778:18:18:72:540:463:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the left panel, the ratio of value-added to gross exports is higher for more distant markets.
10:326:24871:18:18:72:540:445:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This  is  reflected  in  the  separate  distance  coefficients  for  gross  versus  value-added  exports
10:327:24975:18:18:72:540:427:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in  the  right  panel.   While  distance  depresses  both,  gross  exports  fall  more  strongly  with
10:328:25077:18:18:72:540:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distance  than  do  value-added  exports  –  i.e.,  the  absolute  value  of  the  distance  coefficient
10:329:25181:18:18:72:540:391:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on gross exports is larger than the coefficient on value-added exports in all years.  Further,
10:330:25275:18:18:72:540:373:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this differential impact of distance is strengthening over time.  The distance coefficient for
10:331:25369:18:18:72:539:355:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the value-added to export ratio has risen from under 0.1 to 0.2.  The reason is that distance
10:332:25462:18:34:72:539:337:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:coefficient for gross exports has risen (in absolute value) over time, from roughly 0.9 to 1.1.14
10:333:25559:34:18:72:539:303:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Regional Trade AgreementsWe now examine how value-added and gross exports re-
10:334:25636:18:18:72:345:285:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:spond to the adoption of regional trade agreements.15
10:335:25689:18:18:89:539:267:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To demonstrate the main result visually, we take an event study approach.  We compare
10:336:25774:18:18:72:540:249:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the evolution of the value-added to export ratio for the treatment group of bilateral country
10:337:25867:18:18:72:540:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:pairs that form new RTA’s during our sample to outcomes for a pair-specific control group
10:338:25956:18:18:72:539:213:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in a window surrounding adoption of the RTA. For country pair (i,j) that forms an RTA,
10:339:26042:18:16:81:539:195:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:13Very  small  bilateral  gross  trade  flows  are  often  lead  to  extreme  value-added  to  export  ratios,  which
10:340:26159:16:12:72:540:179:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:distort the point estimates and muddy inference.  These outliers are mostly for emerging markets with low
10:341:26264:12:12:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:quality data during the 1970-1985 period.  We remove them by dropping bilateral flows less than one million
10:342:26371:12:8:72:349:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:dollars and value-added to gross export ratios greater than ten.
10:343:26435:8:16:81:539:147:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:14In  the  Appendix,  we  show  that  changes  in  these  coefficients  are  robust  to  adding  additional  gravity
10:344:26551:16:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:controls (e.g., indicators for common language, border, and colonial origin) and country-pair fixed effects.
10:345:26659:12:12:72:540:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Further, distance stands out among gravity variables in terms of its ability to explain changes in the value-
10:346:26768:12:8:72:168:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:added to export ratio.
10:347:26790:8:16:81:539:99:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:15We  use  data  on  economic  integration  agreements  assembled  by  Scott  Baier  and  Jeffrey  Bergstrand,
10:348:26900:16:12:72:539:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:covering  the  1960-2009  period  [September  2015  Revision;http://www.nd.edu/~jbergstr/].   Our  RTA
10:349:27002:12:0:72:445:71:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:indicator takes the value one if a country pair has a free trade agreement or stronger.
11:350:27090:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the control series is the bilateral value-added to export ratio for countriesiandjvis-`a-vis
11:351:27182:18:18:71:412:690:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:the set of countries with whom bothiandjnever form an RTA.16
11:352:27242:18:18:89:539:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We plot the resulting treatment and control series in Figure 5.  Prior to RTA adoption,
11:353:27329:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added to export ratios are quite similar across the treatment and control groups.  There
11:354:27423:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is then a strong divergence between the two, coinciding with adoption of the RTA: for pairs
11:355:27514:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that adopt an RTA, the value-added to export ratio drops sharply around the adoption date
11:356:27603:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and then continues to fall for roughly a decade thereafter.  This divergence is prima facie
11:357:27694:18:18:72:520:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:evidence that trade agreements have different effects on gross versus value-added trade.
11:358:27782:18:18:89:539:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To formalize these results and control for confounding factors, we turn to panel regressions
11:359:27874:18:18:72:132:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of the form:
11:360:27886:18:26:162:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:log(yijt) =φyit+φyjt+φyij+βyTradeAgreementijt+εyijt,(4)
11:361:27941:26:18:72:540:502:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:whereTradeAgreementijtis a collection of indicators for whetheriandjare in a particular
11:362:28028:18:18:72:539:484:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade agreement at timetandφyijis a country-pair fixed effect.17We consider several different
11:363:28121:18:17:72:539:466:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:specifications forTradeAgreementijt.  The first uses a single indicator for whether countries
11:364:28214:17:18:72:540:449:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have an RTA in force, the second distinguishes “shallow” from “deep” agreements, and the
11:365:28302:18:18:72:540:431:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:third allows for phase-in effects.18We estimate this equation using data at five-year intervals
11:366:28397:18:18:72:360:413:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 1970 to 2009 (the 2005-2009 interval is four years).
11:367:28454:18:18:89:539:395:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Table 1 reports the estimation results.  We find that adoption of trade agreements low-
11:368:28541:18:18:72:540:377:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ers value-added relative to gross exports among countries in those agreements.  Using the
11:369:28630:18:18:72:540:359:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simple  binary  RTA  indicator  (RTAijt),  the  ratio  falls  by  about  5%  following  adoption  of
11:370:28730:18:18:72:540:341:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:an agreement.  In columns 3 and 4, we split out the effects of different agreements.  While
11:371:28821:18:18:72:540:323:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:signing a preferential agreement has no impact on the ratio of value-added to gross exports,
11:372:28913:18:18:72:540:305:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:adoption  of  an  FTA  lowers  the  ratio  of  value-added  to  gross  exports.   Further,  “deeper”
11:373:29013:18:18:72:540:287:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:CUCMEU agreements are associated with larger declines than FTAs.  Following adoption
11:374:29097:18:18:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of a CUCMEU, the ratio declines between 8-12%, whereas adoption of a FTA is associated
11:375:29183:18:19:72:540:251:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with a drop of 4-5%.  The response of gross and value-added trade flows to RTA adoption
11:376:29270:19:7:81:251:232:7:g_d0_f5:5:g_d0_f19:A:16Formally, defineTt(i,j) =vaijt+vajit
11:377:29308:7:10:214:539:225:7:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f6:F:xijt+xjitto be the bilateral value-added to export ratio for (i,j) pairs in
11:378:29383:10:14:72:539:215:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the treatment group.  Further, letK(i,j) denote the set of countries with whom bothiandjnever form
11:379:29481:14:0:72:203:201:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:an RTA, and defineCt(i,j) =
11:380:29508:0:11:207:218:209:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f11:?:∑
11:381:29509:11:0:217:248:198:7:g_d0_f18:7:g_d0_f5:?:c∈K(i,j)
11:382:29517:0:8:252:355:206:7:g_d0_f5:7:g_d0_f5:?:(vacjt+vajct)+(vacit+vaict)
11:383:29544:8:11:260:540:198:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:(xcjt+xjct)+(xcit+xict)to be the value-added to export ratio for
11:384:29608:11:12:71:540:187:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:trade betweeniandjwith the control group.  Ift= 0 is the year of RTA adoption, then we computeTt(i,j)
11:385:29709:12:8:71:527:175:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:andCt(i,j) fort= [−20,20] for each pair and take an unweighted average of each series across all pairs.
11:386:29812:8:16:81:539:167:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:17As discussed by Baier and Bergstrand (2007), the pair fixed effect accounts for endogenous adoption of
11:387:29916:16:11:71:539:151:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:agreements based on time-invariant characteristics of the bilateral pair.  In some specifications, we also add
11:388:30026:11:12:71:540:140:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:a pair-specific linear trend (δyijt), which controls for endogenous adoption based on trending characteristics.
11:389:30137:12:9:71:438:128:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:These controls also absorb pair-specific levels and trends in unmeasured trade costs.
11:390:30222:9:15:81:539:119:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:18In terms of depth,  we distinguish preferential trade agreements (PTA), free trade agreements (FTA),
11:391:30324:15:12:71:539:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and customs unions, common markets, and economic unions (CUCMEU). To allow for phase-in effects, we
11:392:30423:12:12:71:540:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:define a set of indicator variables:RTAijt(1) takes the value 1 in the RTA adoption year,RTAijt(2) equals
11:393:30528:12:0:71:530:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:1 in the 5th year,RTAijt(3) equals 1 in the tenth year, andRTAijt(4) equals 1 one for years 15 onward.
12:394:30632:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are reported in Panels B and C. Consistent with the changes in value-added to export ratios,
12:395:30724:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we find that gross exports rise more following the adoption of RTAs than do value-added
12:396:30811:18:18:72:351:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exports, with larger differences for “deep” agreements.
12:397:30866:18:18:89:539:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To quantify adjustment dynamics, we report the coefficients on RTA indicators for specific
12:398:30956:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:periods post-RTA adoption in columns 5 and 6 of Table 1.  Consistent with the dynamics in
12:399:31045:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Figure 5, the impact of RTA adoption appears to grow over time.19Upon adoption of the
12:400:31130:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:RTA, the ratio of value-added to gross exports falls by 3-5% and then continue to fall over
12:401:31221:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the duration of the agreement.  The total effect levels off at around 9-11%.  Value added and
12:402:31314:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gross exports follow similar adjustment dynamics, with value-added exports rising between
12:403:31403:18:39:72:405:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:28-39% and gross exports rising between 37-50% in the long run.
12:404:31466:39:25:72:347:507:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:2.3    Summing Up:  Five Stylized Facts
12:405:31505:25:18:72:540:482:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To  sum  up,  we  have  documented  five  stylized  facts.   The  first  is  that  the  ratio  of  world
12:406:31609:18:18:72:540:464:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added  to  gross  exports  has  fallen  over  time,  by  roughly  ten  percentage  points  and
12:407:31709:18:18:72:540:446:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mostly post-1990.  The second is that the ratio of value-added to gross exports has fallen for
12:408:31803:18:18:72:540:428:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing, but actually risen outside of manufacturing.  The third fact is that changes
12:409:31894:18:18:72:540:410:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:have been heterogeneous across countries, with fast growing countries seeing larger declines
12:410:31986:18:18:72:540:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the ratio of their value-added to gross exports.  The fourth and fifth facts concern bilateral
12:411:32083:18:18:72:540:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes:  declines in value-added to export ratios have been larger for proximate partners
12:412:32173:18:45:72:372:356:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and country pairs that adopted regional trade agreements.
12:413:32230:45:30:72:478:311:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:3    What Driving Forces Account for the Facts?
12:414:32277:30:18:72:540:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we ask:  what driving forces account for the five stylized facts identified in
12:415:32372:18:18:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Section 2?  Are they products of distinct driving forces, or do common driving forces account
12:416:32465:18:18:72:540:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for multiple facts simultaneously?  These questions are difficult to answer with data alone.
12:417:32557:18:18:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Many features of the global economy have changed over time, and these changes are linked
12:418:32645:18:18:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:together: any given candidate driving force (e.g., changes in productivity, endowments, trade
12:419:32738:18:17:72:540:191:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions, etc.)  leads to changes in multiple aspects of the input-output system.  Therefore,
12:420:32832:17:18:72:540:174:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we need a structural equilibrium framework to disentangle competing explanations for the
12:421:32920:18:18:72:141:156:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:stylized facts.
12:422:32935:18:18:89:539:138:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We develop an Armington-style model with cross-sector and cross-country input-output
12:423:33019:18:16:81:539:120:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:19Adjustment dynamics may arise for several reasons.  First, trade agreements are typically phased in, so
12:424:33124:16:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:impact of the agreement may grows over time.  Second, it may take time for trade flows to respond to those
12:425:33230:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:changes.  Third, countries with weaker agreements may adopt stronger agreements at a later date, so the
12:426:33333:12:0:72:304:80:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:depth of liberalization evolves over time within pairs.
13:427:33390:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:linkages suited to this task.  The analysis then proceeds in two steps.  First, we use the model
13:428:33486:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:as a measurement device.  While we are able to collect data on some driving forces (e.g.,
13:429:33575:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes in factor inputs and productivity across countries), it is difficult to directly measure
13:430:33671:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:others.  Specifically, trade costs are difficult to measure in a comprehensive, consistent way
13:431:33765:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:through time [Anderson and van Wincoop (2004)].  Changes in preferences and production
13:432:33851:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:technologies needed to match final and input expenditure across sectors are also unobserved.
13:433:33943:18:18:72:374:600:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:Therefore, we combine the model and data to infer them.20
13:434:34000:18:18:89:539:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second,  we  apply  the  model  in  a  series  of  counterfactuals  to  assign  responsibility  to
13:435:34098:18:18:71:539:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:particular driving forces for changes in value-added to gross exports in the data.  To preview
13:436:34192:18:18:71:539:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the main result, we demonstrate that changes in trade frictions provide a unifying explanation
13:437:34286:18:18:71:539:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for all five stylized facts.  Following up on this result, we use the model to study the role of
13:438:34382:18:39:71:428:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:particular components of trade frictions in greater detail in Section 4.
13:439:34454:39:25:71:186:471:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:3.1    Framework
13:440:34470:25:18:71:539:446:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This section lays out the core elements of the model.  Details regarding the model and its
13:441:34560:18:35:71:270:428:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:solution are collected in the Appendix.

15:538:38895:35:25:71:195:565:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:3.1.2    Quantification
15:539:38918:25:18:71:539:540:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To operationalize the model, we need to assign values to model parameters and collect data
15:540:39008:18:18:71:539:522:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:on exogenous forcing variables.  Further, we need to back changes in unknown parameters
15:541:39095:18:18:71:539:504:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(e.g.,  trade  frictions,  preference/technology  parameters)  out  of  the  data.   We  provide  an
15:542:39195:18:18:71:539:486:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:overview of the procedure here, with details in the Appendix.  As a practical matter, we use
15:543:39287:18:18:71:539:468:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a two-sector version of the model and data to simplify the parametrization,  computation,
15:544:39376:18:18:71:539:450:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and counterfactual analysis.  The two sectors are manufacturing (m) and non-manufacturing
15:545:39465:18:35:71:197:432:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(n) sectors (s∈{m,n}).
15:546:39487:35:17:71:539:397:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Parameters  and  Exogenous  VariablesThe  parameterκgoverns  substitution  across
15:547:39568:17:18:72:539:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries  in  final  and  intermediate  input  purchases.   We  setκ=  0.75  –  corresponding  to
15:548:39666:18:18:71:539:362:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:an  elasticity  of  substitution  of  4  –  to  match  standard  estimates  of  the  trade  elasticity.23
15:549:39771:18:18:71:539:344:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The parameterρgoverns the cross-sector elasticity of substitution in final demand,  while
15:550:39860:18:18:71:539:326:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:B:σgoverns substitution between real value added and inputs, as well as across inputs from
15:551:39948:18:18:71:539:308:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:different sectors.  We setσ=ρ=−1, corresponding to an elasticity of substitution equal
15:552:40034:18:18:71:539:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to 0.5.  This means that manufacturing and non-manufacturing sectors are complements in
15:553:40121:18:18:71:539:272:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:final demand, and that value added and inputs, and inputs across sectors, are complements
15:554:40210:18:18:71:512:254:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:on the production side.  These assumptions are both supported by existing evidence.24
15:555:40295:18:18:89:540:236:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We  also  need  values  for  the  parameters{α,δit}and  the  exogenous  forcing  variables
15:556:40385:18:18:71:540:218:12:g_d0_f14:12:g_d0_f2:B:{ˆsit,ˆTit,ˆZit(s),ˆLit}.  We setα= 0.3, and we set the remaining values based on our input-
15:557:40477:18:18:71:280:200:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:output data and the Penn World Tables.
15:558:40515:18:15:81:539:182:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:23This is the mean SITC 3-digit trade elasticity reported in Broda and Weinstein (2006).  Note that we
15:559:40617:15:12:72:539:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:implicitly restrictκto be the same in both sectors.  This is consistent with the fact that the mean Broda-
15:560:40723:12:12:71:539:155:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Weinstein elasticity  estimates  for non-manufacturing (SITC  1-4) and  manufacturing (SITC  5-8) are  both
15:561:40830:12:8:71:115:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:close to 4.
15:562:40841:8:16:81:539:135:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:24The low final expenditure elasticity is consistent with the fact that both the share of non-manufacturing
15:563:40948:16:12:71:539:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:in final expenditure and the relative price of non-manufacturing output have been rising over time.  This
15:564:41053:12:12:71:539:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:observation motivates low elasticities in the structural change literature [Ngai and Pissarides (2007), Herren-
15:565:41164:12:12:71:539:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:dorf, Rogerson and Valentinyi (2013)].  Low substitutability in production is consistent with recent estimates
15:566:41274:12:0:71:288:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:by Atalay (2015) and Oberfield and Raval (2015).
16:567:41324:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Trade Frictions and Expenditure WeightsWe introduce the following notation and
16:568:41402:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:nomenclature to clarify what aspects of trade frictions and preference/technology parame-
16:569:41491:18:18:72:539:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ters we are able to back out of the data.25First, we normalize international iceberg frictions
16:570:41585:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relative  to  domestic  frictions:ωXjit(s′,s)≡τXjit(s′,s)/τXiit(s′,s)  andωFjit(s)≡τFjit(s)/τFiit(s).
16:571:41686:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Henceforth,  we  refer  toωXjit(s′,s)  andωFjit(s)  as  “trade  frictions,”  since  they  govern  sub-
16:572:41788:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:stitution  across  country  sources.   Given  this  normalization  of  the  international  frictions,
16:573:41889:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we define a second set of “expenditure weights” that combine CES weight parameters and
16:574:41975:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:domestic frictions:ωXit(s′,s)≡λXit(s′,s)(σ−1)/στXiit(s′,s),ωFit(s)≡λFit(s)(ρ−1)/ρτFiit(s).  These
16:575:42072:18:18:72:531:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expenditure weights govern substitution across sectors in sourcing inputs and final goods.
16:576:42162:18:18:89:539:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To compute changes in trade frictions and expenditure weights, we need additional price
16:577:42249:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data, not already included in our global input-output data.  We need changes in the price
16:578:42338:18:18:72:539:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of real value added in each sector  ˆpVi(s), and changes in the price of final expenditure  ˆpFi.
16:579:42435:18:18:72:539:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Drawing on national accounts sources, we set  ˆpVi(s) equal to changes in GDP deflators in
16:580:42525:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the manufacturing and non-manufacturing sectors,  and we set  ˆpFiequal to changes in the
16:581:42614:17:18:72:539:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratio of nominal to real final expenditure.26We use this price data, along with first order
16:582:42705:18:18:72:540:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:conditions and price indexes from the model, to solve for changes in the trade frictions and
16:583:42797:18:18:72:444:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expenditure weights implied by changes in expenditure shares over time.
16:584:42868:18:18:89:539:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The  estimates  we  recover  via  this  procedure  are  sensible  and  consistent  with  related
16:585:42964:18:18:72:539:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:work.27Globally, iceberg trade frictions decline by 35% from 1970-2009.  There is significant
16:586:43057:18:18:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dispersion in the magnitude of the declines across countries, and declines are strongly corre-
16:587:43151:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lated with changes in openness (as expected).  Trade frictions decline both in manufacturing
16:588:43243:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and non-manufacturing sectors, though declines are somewhat larger for manufactures.  As
16:589:43331:18:18:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we discuss further in Section 4.2, RTA adoption predicts declines in trade frictions at the
16:590:43422:18:18:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bilateral level.  As for the expenditure weights, changes in final goods expenditure weights
16:591:43514:18:18:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are  similar  across  manufacturing  and  non-manufacturing  sectors,  while  input  expenditure
16:592:43610:18:39:72:452:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weights have tended to pull input expenditure toward non-manufacturing.
16:593:43681:39:25:72:220:220:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:3.2    Counterfactuals
16:594:43703:25:18:72:540:195:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  now  conduct  counterfactuals  in  the  model  to  assess  the  role  of  trade  frictions  in  ex-
16:595:43806:18:18:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:plaining changes in value-added to export ratios.  The first counterfactual holds both trade
16:596:43898:18:16:81:539:159:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:25If domestic trade frictions are constant, then we could identify changes in the level of international trade
16:597:44008:16:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:frictions and changes in the CES expenditure weights in preferences and technologies.  We do not assume
16:598:44111:12:8:72:395:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:that domestic frictions are constant, so we must redefine parameters here.
16:599:44185:8:15:81:539:123:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:26We take price changes and exchange rates (to convert GDP and final expenditure deflators to a common
16:600:44287:15:12:72:539:108:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:currency) from the UN National Accounts Database, with one exception.  For China, we take  ˆpVi(s) from
16:601:44390:12:9:72:467:96:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the World Development Indicators, since it is missing for many years in the UN Database.
16:602:44478:9:15:81:539:87:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:27For example, our estimated changes in trade frictions are comparable to estimates by Jacks, Meissner
16:603:44580:15:0:72:332:72:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and Novy (2011).  See the Appendix for detailed discussion.
17:604:44641:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions and expenditure weights constant at their 1970 levels. Following our notation above,
17:605:44735:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f14:B:ˆωXit(s′,s) = ˆωFit(s) = ˆωXjit(s′,s) = ˆωFjit(s) = 1, and the exogenous variables{ˆsit,ˆTit,ˆZit(s),ˆLit}
17:606:44841:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:evolve as in the data.  We treat this simulation as the baseline against which we assess the
17:607:44933:18:18:72:360:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relative importance of trade versus expenditure weights.
17:608:44989:18:18:89:539:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The second and third counterfactuals decompose the gap between this baseline and the
17:609:45073:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data into components due to trade frictions versus expenditure weights.  The second coun-
17:610:45162:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:terfactual holds expenditure weights constant at their 1970 levels (ˆωXit(s′,s) =  ˆωFit(s) = 1)
17:611:45258:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and allows trade frictions to evolve as implied by the data.  The third counterfactual holds
17:612:45350:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade frictions constant at their 1970 levels (ˆωXjit(s′,s) = ˆωFjit(s) = 1) and allows expenditure
17:613:45449:18:18:72:282:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weights to evolve as implied by the data.
17:614:45490:18:18:89:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure  6  plots  changes  in  value-added  to  export  ratios  for  the  world  as  a  whole.28In
17:615:45588:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the baseline simulation of the model,  there is virtually no long run change in the ratio of
17:616:45680:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added to gross exports.  This means that changes in country size and sectoral output
17:617:45770:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:composition, driven by both productivity and factor endowments in the baseline simulation,
17:618:45860:17:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:do not explain the decline in the ratio of value-added to gross exports.  Interestingly, these
17:619:45954:18:18:72:540:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:results obtain even though world trade does rise substantially in this counterfactual simu-
17:620:46045:18:18:72:540:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lation:  the baseline model alone accounts for about 40% of the growth of trade since 1970.
17:621:46136:18:18:72:540:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The stability of the value-added to export ratio simply means that this gross trade growth
17:622:46226:18:18:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is matched by increases in value-added exports.  Adding expenditure weights to the model
17:623:46314:18:18:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:does little to change this result.  In contrast, allowing trade frictions to change generates a
17:624:46409:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simulated series that captures the evolution of the ratio of value-added to gross exports well.
17:625:46504:18:18:89:539:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 7 plots changes in value-added to export ratios at the sector level.  Here again,
17:626:46592:18:18:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the baseline simulation performs poorly.  It accounts for only a small part of the declining
17:627:46684:18:18:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added  to  export  ratio  in  manufacturing,  and  the  ratio  in  non-manufacturing  moves
17:628:46781:18:18:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the wrong direction entirely.  Expenditure weights close the gap between simulation and
17:629:46871:18:18:72:540:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data,  but their explanatory power is limited.  Where they play a role is in explaining the
17:630:46962:18:17:72:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:medium-term dynamics of the manufacturing VAX ratio, capturing the pre-1990 slowdown
17:631:47046:17:18:72:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and post-1990 acceleration in the rate of decline.  Trade frictions are important in explaining
17:632:47141:18:18:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes in the VAX ratio for both sectors.  They explain changes in the non-manufacturing
17:633:47230:18:18:72:540:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sector almost completely, and explain more than half of the steady decline in manufacturing.
17:634:47322:18:18:89:539:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Figure  8  plots  changes  in  value-added  to  export  ratios  from  1975-2005  at  the  country
17:635:47419:18:18:72:540:152:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:level.  As in previous figures, both the baseline simulation and the simulation with changes
17:636:47511:18:18:72:540:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in expenditure weights struggle to match the data.  The baseline simulation under-predicts
17:637:47601:18:18:72:540:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the magnitude of the declines,  particularly for countries with large declines.  Expenditure
17:638:47693:18:15:81:539:98:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:28To match the simulation, the ‘true’ value-added to export ratios in these figures are computed using a
17:639:47797:15:0:72:317:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:thirty-seven country, two-sector aggregation of the data.
18:640:47856:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weights generate additional dispersion, but do little to improve the overall fit. Again, changes
18:641:47952:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in trade frictions bring the simulated data in line with actual changes for most countries.
18:642:48043:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The visual impression conferred by the figures is confirmed by more systematic measures of
18:643:48133:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the goodness of fit.  The correlations between simulations and data are 0.39 in panels (a)
18:644:48223:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and (b), and 0.69 in panel (c).  Mean errors are 0.11, 0.08, and 0.03 for the three simulations
18:645:48318:18:18:72:125:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(in order).
18:646:48329:18:18:89:539:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Turning to bilateral flows, we estimate the same regressions used to describe the stylized
18:647:48419:18:18:72:540:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:facts in Section 2 using simulated data.  The left panel of Table 2 includes long difference
18:648:48511:18:18:72:540:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:regressions that focus on the role distance, while the right panel examines the role of RTAs.
18:649:48604:18:18:89:539:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Consistent with the discussion above, the baseline simulation fails badly at explaining the
18:650:48695:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distance or RTA results.  Input-output and final goods expenditure weights do a little better
18:651:48788:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for the distance effects.  However, it looks initially like they help explain declines in the value-
18:652:48888:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added to export ratio surrounding RTAs.  This apparent good performance is misleading.
18:653:48974:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Looking at Panels B and C, changes in input-output and final goods expenditure weights
18:654:49060:17:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:lower the simulated bilateral VAX ratio because they lead bilateral value-added exports to
18:655:49150:18:18:72:540:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:fall post-RTA adoption, while gross exports are unchanged – both these results are grossly
18:656:49240:18:18:72:540:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:inconsistent with the data.  Changes in trade frictions, instead, do well in matching both the
18:657:49334:18:18:72:540:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distance and RTA evidence.  They predict both the decline in the VAX ratio and the rise in
18:658:49424:18:18:72:332:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added and gross exports post-RTA adoption.
18:659:49472:18:18:89:539:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To sum up, we set out to evaluate the role of trade frictions in explaining the data.  The
18:660:49562:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:counterfactuals point to changes in trade frictions as the most important force underlying all
18:661:49656:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:five stylized facts.  We therefore shift our attention to examining the role of trade frictions
18:662:49751:18:45:72:157:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in greater detail.
18:663:49769:45:30:72:439:268:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:4    Interpreting the Role of Trade Frictions
18:664:49814:30:18:72:540:238:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To  interpret  the  role  of  trade  frictions  in  explaining  the  five  facts,  we  unpack  the  fric-
18:665:49919:18:18:72:540:220:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tions  themselves.   First,  we  distinguish  trade  frictions  by  sector  (manufacturing  vs.  non-
18:666:50020:18:18:72:540:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing)  and  end  use  (final  vs.  intermediate  goods).   We  simulate  the  model  with
18:667:50118:18:18:72:540:184:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:each set of frictions independently, and we use these simulations together with accounting
18:668:50208:18:17:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:relationships  to  examine  the  mechanics  underlying  changes  in  multilateral  value-added  to
18:669:50306:17:18:72:540:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:export ratios.  Second, we hone in on bilateral frictions to examine the role of policy changes
18:670:50401:18:0:72:488:131:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:– specifically, RTA adoption – in driving changes in value-added to export ratios.
19:671:50485:0:26:72:296:708:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:4.1    Unpacking Trade Frictions
19:672:50517:26:18:72:540:682:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  run  four  new  counterfactual  simulations  with  changes  in  (1)  final  non-manufacturing
19:673:50614:18:18:72:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade frictions, holding other trade frictions fixed (ˆωFjit(m) = ˆωXjit(s′,s) = 1), (2) input non-
19:674:50713:18:18:72:540:646:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing trade frictions (with  ˆωFjit(s) = ˆωXjit(m,s) = 1), (3) final manufacturing trade
19:675:50809:18:18:72:540:628:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions (with  ˆωFjit(n) =  ˆωXjit(s′,s) = 1), and (4) input manufacturing trade frictions (with
19:676:50907:18:18:72:539:610:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ˆωFjit(s)  =  ˆωXjit(n,s)  =  1).   In  all  these  simulations,  expenditure  weights  are  held  constant
19:677:51014:18:18:72:539:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:at 1970 levels (ˆωXit(s′,s) =  ˆωFit(s)).  These four simulations decompose the Trade Frictions
19:678:51109:18:34:72:540:574:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:simulation presented previously, so we compare them that simulation in the analysis below.
19:679:51199:34:26:72:156:540:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:4.1.1    Results
19:680:51215:26:18:72:540:514:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Along  all  three  dimensions  of  the  multilateral  data  –  for  the  world,  countries,  and  sec-
19:681:51317:18:18:72:540:496:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tors  –  trade  frictions  for  manufactures  are  more  important  than  trade  frictions  for  non-
19:682:51418:18:18:72:540:478:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing  output.   Further,  among  manufacturing  trade  frictions,  frictions  for  inputs
19:683:51516:18:18:72:325:460:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are more important than frictions for final goods.
19:684:51566:18:18:89:539:442:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Figure 9, we plot the four simulations for the world.  As is evident, changes in trade
19:685:51655:18:18:72:540:424:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions for manufacturing goods used as inputs generate a large decline in the simulated
19:686:51745:18:17:72:540:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added to export ratio.  While changes in the remaining frictions are less important,
19:687:51835:17:18:72:540:389:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the ratio also declines due to changes in frictions for final manufacturing goods (particularly
19:688:51930:18:18:72:129:371:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:post-1990).
19:689:51941:18:18:89:539:353:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Turning to sectors, we plot value-added to export ratios in the four simulations in Figure
19:690:52031:18:18:72:540:335:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:10.  Here again, manufacturing trade frictions do the bulk of the work.  Frictions on manu-
19:691:52122:18:18:72:540:317:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:factured inputs are most important in accounting for the decline in the manufacturing ratio,
19:692:52214:18:18:72:540:299:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:though frictions on final manufactures matter as well.  For the non-manufacturing sector,
19:693:52303:18:18:72:540:281:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:final  manufacturing  frictions  are  most  important,  with  input-manufacturing  frictions  also
19:694:52401:18:18:72:481:263:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:playing a role in explaining the rising value-added to export ratio in this sector.
19:695:52484:18:18:89:539:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:At the country level, all four simulations generate data that is positively correlated with
19:696:52575:18:18:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the composite Trade Frictions simulation, and roughly equally so (the correlations are all near
19:697:52670:18:18:72:540:209:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:0.5).  However, simulations with changes in non-manufacturing frictions alone underpredict
19:698:52760:18:18:72:540:191:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the size of the declines in value-added to export ratios:  the mean error is 0.10 in simulation
19:699:52855:18:18:72:540:173:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(1)  and  0.08  in  simulation  (2).   In  contrast,  simulations  with  changes  in  manufacturing
19:700:52954:18:17:72:540:155:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions generate significant declines in value-added to export ratios, with a mean error of
19:701:53047:17:18:72:540:138:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:0.04 in simulation (3) and -0.006 in simulation (4).  From this, we conclude that frictions for
19:702:53142:18:0:72:495:120:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufactured inputs are most important in explaining country-level ratios as well.
20:703:53226:0:26:72:196:708:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f8:C:4.1.2    Interpretation
20:704:53249:26:18:72:540:682:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To  interpret  these  results,  we  combine  accounting  results  with  the  intuitive  mechanics  of
20:705:53350:18:18:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the model.  We start by explaining the link between sector-level trends and the world-level
20:706:53441:18:18:72:540:646:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:results,  and then we discuss sector trends themselves in detail.  We conclude with a brief
20:707:53532:18:35:72:296:628:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:discussion of heterogeneity across countries.
20:708:53577:35:17:72:540:593:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Aggregating Sector TrendsThe world value-added to export ratio can be written as a
20:709:53659:17:0:72:350:576:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade-weighted average of sector-level ratios:V AXt=
20:710:53711:0:12:354:367:584:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:A:∑
20:711:53712:12:0:366:370:572:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:s
20:712:53713:0:8:373:380:589:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:(
20:713:53714:8:10:381:402:581:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f3:A:Xt(s)

20:716:53722:13:18:412:540:576:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:V AXit(s).  This suggests
20:717:53747:18:18:72:540:558:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a simple Between-Within decomposition of changes inV AXtbetween yeartand yeart−1
20:718:53827:18:33:72:447:540:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:into components due to changes inV AXt(s) versus sectoral trade shares:
20:719:53898:33:0:168:221:507:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:∆V AXt=

20:721:53906:25:0:231:235:493:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:s
20:722:53907:0:19:245:326:507:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A: ̄ωt(s)∆V AXt(s)
20:723:53923:19:11:225:325:488:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:A:︸︷︷︸

20:725:53933:0:0:327:336:507:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:+
20:726:53934:0:25:338:355:518:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:∑
20:727:53935:25:0:345:349:493:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:s

20:729:53950:19:11:338:440:488:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:︸︷︷︸
20:730:53954:11:0:374:405:477:8:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f3:?:Between
20:731:53961:0:60:440:540:507:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:,(11)
20:732:53966:60:0:71:447:447:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:where ∆ denotes time differences,  ̄ωt(s)≡12(ωt(s) +ωt−1(s)) withωt(s) =
