20:737:54056:9:0:482:512:440:6:g_d0_f15:8:g_d0_f3:?:sxijt(s)
20:738:54064:0:20:514:540:447:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:, and
20:739:54069:20:18:72:271:427:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:B:V AXt(s)≡12(V AXt(s) +V AXt−1(s)).
20:740:54103:18:18:89:539:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:We report this decomposition in actual and simulated data in Table 3.  In the data, both
20:741:54191:18:17:72:540:391:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the between and within terms are negative, and the within term is larger than the between
20:742:54280:17:18:72:540:374:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:term. The within term is obviously driven by the decline in the manufacturing value-added to
20:743:54372:18:18:72:540:356:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:export ratio, which dominates the rise in the non-manufacturing ratio because manufactures
20:744:54462:18:18:72:540:338:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:account  for  60-70%  of  world  trade.   The  between  term  is  negative  because  the  share  of
20:745:54561:18:18:72:540:320:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing rises over the period, and the level of the value-added to export ratio is lower
20:746:54655:18:18:72:540:302:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in  manufacturing  than  non-manufacturing.   The  between  term  is  modest  in  size  because
20:747:54750:18:18:72:378:284:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:changes in trade shares are small (a few percentage points).
20:748:54810:18:18:89:539:266:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Turning  to  simulation  results,  the  Trade  Frictions  counterfactual  generates  negative
20:749:54903:18:18:72:540:248:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:within  and  between  effects,  consistent  with  the  data.   Breaking  the  trade  frictions  apart,
20:750:55005:18:18:72:540:230:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:only the simulation with declines in trade frictions for manufactures used as inputs matches
20:751:55097:18:18:72:540:212:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this pattern (and hence the data).  All the other trade frictions simulations generate results
20:752:55191:18:18:72:540:194:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:that point in the wrong direction for explaining the data, in one way or another.29Falling
20:753:55281:18:17:71:539:176:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:input frictions for manufacturing goods lower the value-added to export ratio in manufac-
20:754:55370:17:16:81:539:159:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:29Non-manufacturing trade costs generate negative within effects, but (counterfactual) positive between
20:755:55473:16:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:effects.  The between effects arise because declines in non-manufacturing trade frictions raise the share of non-
20:756:55586:12:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:manufacturing in trade, and non-manufacturing has a higher value-added to export ratio than manufacturing.
20:757:55692:12:12:72:540:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Declining trade frictions for final manufactures generate strong negative between effects, but positive within
20:758:55802:12:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:effects.  The negative between effect here arises because declines in manufacturing frictions raise the share
20:759:55911:12:12:72:540:95:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:of  manufactures  in  trade.   The  positive  within  effect  is  a  product  of  the  strong  rise  in  VAX  ratios  for
20:760:56032:12:0:72:405:83:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:non-manufacturing in response to falling final manufacturing trade frictions.
21:761:56111:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:turing (as in Figure 10), and this drives the within effect.  They also increase the share of
21:762:56204:18:35:72:433:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufactures in trade, which accounts for the negative between effect.
21:763:56274:35:18:72:539:655:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Sector  TrendsWe  now  turn  our  attention  to  explaining  the  sector  trends  themselves.
21:764:56367:18:18:71:539:637:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To  do  this,  it  is  helpful  to  appeal  to  the  tight  (empirical  and  conceptual)  link  between
21:765:56470:18:18:71:539:619:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added  to  export  ratios  and  the  domestic  content  of  exports.   Domestic  content  is
21:766:56568:18:18:71:539:601:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the  value  of  exports  less  the  import  content  of  exports,  and  it  measures  the  amount  of
21:767:56669:18:18:71:539:583:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:domestic  value  added  needed  to  produce  exports  [Hummels,  Ishii  and  Yi  (2001)].   As  an
21:768:56767:18:18:71:539:565:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:empirical matter, sector-level domestic content is approximately equal to sector-level value-
21:769:56860:18:18:71:539:547:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added  exports  [Johnson  and  Noguera  (2012a)].   We  therefore  use  domestic  content  as  a
21:770:56956:18:17:72:540:529:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proxy for value-added exports in interpreting sector trends, because domestic content is a
21:771:57046:17:18:72:294:512:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:mathematically simpler object to interpret.
21:772:57089:18:18:89:539:494:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f10:A:Formally,  domestic  content  can  be  written  asDCit=Rit(I−ADit)−1Xit,  whereRit
21:773:57171:18:18:72:539:476:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f10:B:is  a  matrix  with  sector-level  value-added  to  output  ratiosrit(s)  along  the  diagonal,ADit
21:774:57270:18:18:72:539:458:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is  the  domestic  input-output  matrix,  andXitis  the  export  vector  of  countryi.30The
21:775:57361:18:18:72:539:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:domestic content to gross export ratio at the sector level is then:DCit(s)/Xit(s) =wit(s,s)+
21:776:57453:18:18:72:540:422:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:B:wit(s,s′)(Xit(s′)/Xit(s)), where the weightswit(s,s′) are the elements ofRit(I−ADit)−1and
21:777:57542:18:18:72:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:indicate how much domestic content from sectorsis needed to produce exports from sector
21:778:57629:18:18:72:539:386:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sectors′.   These  weights  depend  both  on  cross-sector  input  linkages  –  how  much  output
21:779:57726:18:18:72:540:368:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from sectorsis used in producing exports in sectors′(via (I−ADit)−1) and the imported-
21:780:57812:18:18:72:540:350:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:input  intensity  of  production  in  sectors(viaRit).   Finally,  note  that  this  ratio  depends
21:781:57911:18:18:72:540:332:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:directly  on  export  composition,  because  domestic  content  is  both  exported  directly  via  a
21:782:58011:18:18:72:540:314:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sector’s own exports and indirectly via exports of other sectors.  As indirect exports rise,
21:783:58103:18:18:72:221:296:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:thenDCit(s)/Xit(s) will rise.
21:784:58132:18:17:89:539:278:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Putting  these  observations  to  work,  falling  frictions  on  manufactured  inputs  increase
21:785:58227:17:18:72:540:261:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:foreign  sourcing  in  the  manufacturing  sector,  and  hence  drive  downDCit(m)/Xit(m)  in
21:786:58320:18:18:72:539:243:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing.  They also simultaneously drive upDCit(n)/Xit(n), because they raise the
21:787:58407:18:18:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:share  of  manufactures  in  trade,  which  in  turn  raises  the  level  of  indirect  exports  by  the
21:788:58511:18:18:72:540:207:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:non-manufacturing  sector.   Falling  frictions  on  final  manufactures  have  similar  composi-
21:789:58608:18:18:72:540:189:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tional  effects,  which  explains  why  they  too  raise  the  value-added  to  export  ratio  in  non-
21:790:58711:18:18:72:540:171:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing.  These compositional effects also contribute to the decline in the value-added
21:791:58804:18:18:72:540:153:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to export ratio in manufacturing, since they lower indirect exports of manufacturing value
21:792:58894:18:18:72:539:135:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added, by reducing (Xit(n)/Xit(m)).  Together these mechanisms explain the dominant role
21:793:58982:18:18:72:540:117:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of manufacturing trade frictions in explaining the trends in sector-level VAX ratios, depicted
21:794:59076:18:15:81:540:99:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:30To interpret this, (I−ADit)−1Xitis the amount of domestic gross output needed to produce exports,
21:795:59175:15:0:72:502:84:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:so multiplying by the value-added to output ratio yields a measure of domestic content in exports.
22:796:59275:0:35:72:132:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in Figure 9.
22:797:59287:35:18:72:539:673:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Country-Level ChangesLooking at cross-country changes in value-added to export ra-
22:798:59369:18:18:72:540:655:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tios,  the same basic within and between forces come into play as in the aggregate.  As at
22:799:59459:18:18:72:540:637:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the world level,  the value-added to export ratio in manufacturing declines in virtually all
22:800:59551:18:18:72:540:619:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries.  The large majority of countries also see increases in value-added to export ratios
22:801:59645:18:18:72:540:601:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in non-manufacturing.  Further, the level of the non-manufacturing ratio is higher than the
22:802:59736:18:18:72:295:583:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufacturing VAX essentially everywhere.
22:803:59777:18:18:89:539:565:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The main difference is that between effects are more important in explaining country-
22:804:59862:18:18:72:540:547:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:level changes than they are at the world level.  The reason is that changes in trade shares
22:805:59953:18:17:72:540:529:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are substantially larger at the country-level than in the aggregate, so they play a larger role
22:806:60048:17:18:72:540:512:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the between-within decomposition.  Countries with the largest declines in value-added to
22:807:60139:18:18:72:540:494:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:export ratios tend to have very large, negative between effects (i.e., tend to see large increases
22:808:60237:18:18:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the share of manufacturing exports).  Because declines in manufacturing trade frictions
22:809:60327:18:18:72:540:458:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:increase manufacturing exports, they are important in explaining cross-country variation as
22:810:60418:18:39:72:95:440:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:well.
22:811:60423:39:26:72:394:401:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:4.2    The Role of Regional Trade Agreements
22:812:60467:26:18:72:540:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  now  turn  to  assessing  the  role  of  regional  trade  agreements  in  our  results.   Our  first
22:813:60571:18:18:72:540:357:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:goal is to confirm that Fact 5 – that RTAs lower bilateral value-added to export ratios –
22:814:60660:18:18:72:540:339:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is  explained  entirely  by  changes  in  bilateral  trade  costs  surrounding  RTA  adoption.   Our
22:815:60760:18:18:72:540:321:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:second goal is to quantify how much of the decline in the world value-added to export ratio
22:816:60851:18:17:72:306:303:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is explained by the spread of RTAs over time.
22:817:60896:17:18:89:539:286:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:The  approach  we  take  is  to  simulate  a  counterfactual  world  in  which  no  new  regional
22:818:60993:18:18:72:540:268:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade agreements were adopted between 1970 and 2009, and then we compare this to data.
22:819:61079:18:18:72:540:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To do this, we need to construct counterfactual trade frictions that capture how trade fric-
22:820:61171:18:18:72:540:232:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tions would have evolved in the absence of post-1970 RTAs.  We proceed in two steps (see
22:821:61259:18:18:72:540:214:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the Appendix for details).  In the first step, we model trade frictions as a function of RTAs
22:822:61352:18:18:72:540:196:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and other unobserved time-varying importer,  exporter,  and pair-specific factors.  We then
22:823:61443:18:18:72:540:178:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:estimate the impact of RTA adoption by regressing changes in measured frictions (for each
22:824:61532:18:18:72:540:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:input sector-pair or final goods sector separately) on fixed effects and trade agreement in-
22:825:61624:18:18:72:540:142:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dicators.  In the second step, we use the estimated RTA coefficients to adjust the measured
22:826:61715:18:18:72:454:124:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:trade frictions, removing changes in trade frictions due to RTA adoption.31
22:827:61790:18:15:81:539:106:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:31For pairs that form a new RTA after 1970, trade frictions equal measured values prior to RTA adoption
22:828:61893:15:12:72:540:91:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:and then are adjusted upward post-RTA as if the RTA were never signed.  For all pairs that either never
22:829:61996:12:0:72:468:79:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:form an RTA or already had an RTA in force in 1970, trade frictions evolve as in the data.
23:830:62088:0:18:89:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:As expected,  our estimates indicate that RTA adoption lowers bilateral trade frictions
23:831:62175:18:18:72:540:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:among adopting countries.32To summarize the magnitudes, trade frictions fall by roughly
23:832:62262:18:18:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:7-8% after RTA adoption when we measure RTA adoption using a simple binary indicator.
23:833:62347:18:18:72:540:654:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:When we allow for dynamic phase-in effects, RTAs lower trade frictions by 16-20% in the
23:834:62434:18:18:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:long run.  We view these estimates as lower and upper bounds on plausible RTA-impacts.
23:835:62520:18:18:72:540:618:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:When we separate agreements, we find an ordering of changes in trade frictions consistent
23:836:62609:18:18:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with our previous gravity results:  deep trade agreements are associated with larger declines
23:837:62702:18:18:72:300:582:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in trade frictions than shallower agreements.
23:838:62747:18:18:89:539:564:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Using these results, we simulate the model with counterfactual trade frictions, removing
23:839:62835:18:18:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:post-1970  RTAs  and  allowing  all  other  driving  forces  (including  changes  in  expenditure
23:840:62932:18:18:72:540:528:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weights) to evolve as implied by data.  This means that deviations between simulated and
23:841:63020:18:18:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:true  data  in  this  set  of  results  reflect  only  the  removal  of  RTAs.   To  frame  the  analysis,
23:842:63126:18:18:72:540:492:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:recall that we showed (in Table 2) that changes in trade frictions can account for declines in
23:843:63220:18:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added relative to gross exports following adoption of regional trade agreements (Fact
23:844:63311:17:18:72:540:457:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:5).  We now demonstrate that changes in bilateral frictions attributable to RTA adoption
23:845:63399:18:18:72:156:439:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:drive this result.
23:846:63417:18:18:89:539:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Using  the  simulated  data,  we  re-estimate  the  baseline  regressions  specifications  used
23:847:63512:18:18:72:540:403:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:previously to document RTA effects.  Because we have removed changes in trade frictions
23:848:63599:18:18:72:539:385:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:attributable to RTAs, we expect RTAs to haveno effecton bilateral value-added to export
23:849:63686:18:18:72:540:367:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratios in these regressions.  In fact, that is exactly what we find.  In Panel A of Table 4, the
23:850:63782:18:18:72:540:349:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:RTA-coefficient is numerically close to, and statistically indistinguishable from, zero.  This
23:851:63876:18:18:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:holds regardless of the technique we use to remove RTAs.  Further, this result reflects zero
23:852:63968:18:18:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:impact  of  regional  agreements  on  both  value-added  exports  (Panel  B)  and  gross  exports
23:853:64065:18:18:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(Panel  C)  when  RTAs  are  removed.   These  results  imply  that  RTA-induced  changes  in
23:854:64158:18:18:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bilateral trade frictions alone drive the differential response of bilateral value-added versus
23:855:64253:18:18:72:378:259:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:gross trade that we observe in the data post-RTA adoption.
23:856:64311:18:17:89:539:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:An important remaining question is: does the spread of RTAs also help explain the global
23:857:64399:17:18:72:540:224:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:decline in the ratio of value-added to gross exports?  The answer is that post-1970 RTAs can
23:858:64491:18:18:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:explain  roughly  6-15%  of  the  overall  decline.   With  RTA  effects  estimated  using  a  binary
23:859:64592:18:18:72:540:188:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:RTA indicator, the ratio of value-added to gross exports for the world as a whole falls by
23:860:64682:18:18:72:540:170:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:about 6% less in the counterfactual than in the data.  When we split agreements by type,
23:861:64770:18:18:72:540:152:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the ratio of value-added to gross exports falls by about 13% less in the counterfactual than
23:862:64862:18:18:72:540:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in the data.  Allowing for phase-in effects, the ratio falls by about 15% less than in the data.
23:863:64958:18:18:72:540:116:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:These magnitudes are sizable given that only about a third of all country pairs adopt RTAs
23:864:65048:18:18:72:540:98:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:during the sample period, and many of these post-1990 agreements have yet to reach their
23:865:65136:18:0:81:344:80:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:A:32See Table C3 of the Appendix for the full set of estimates.
24:866:65199:0:18:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:peak impact.  We conclude that the spread of RTAs has played a significant quantitative role
24:867:65291:18:46:72:424:690:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in driving the global decline in value-added relative to gross exports.
24:868:65362:46:29:72:194:644:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:5    Conclusion
24:869:65377:29:18:72:540:615:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:With the rise of cross-border supply chains, conventional (gross) trade data is an increasingly
24:870:65472:18:18:72:540:597:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:misleading  guide  to  how  value  added  is  traded  in  the  global  economy.   In  this  paper,  we
24:871:65574:18:18:72:540:579:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:characterized  changes  in  gross  versus  value-added  trade  over  four  decades.   Value-added
24:872:65671:18:18:72:540:561:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exports are falling relative to gross exports, implying double counting in gross trade data is
24:873:65765:18:18:72:540:543:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more pervasive today than in the past.  Importantly,  gaps between gross and value-added
24:874:65853:18:18:72:540:525:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:exports are unevenly distributed across time, sectors, countries, and bilateral partners. These
24:875:65948:18:18:72:540:507:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:differences imply that shifting from a gross to value-added view of trade changes the relative
24:876:66042:18:18:72:540:489:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:openness of sectors or countries, and the relative importance of bilateral trade partners for
24:877:66135:18:18:72:154:471:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a given country.
24:878:66151:18:18:89:539:453:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Using a structural gravity model, we found that changes in trade frictions play a first-
24:879:66239:18:18:72:540:435:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:order role in explaining not only global trends, but also differences across countries, sectors,
24:880:66335:18:17:72:540:417:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and bilateral partners. We emphasize in particular that regional trade agreements have led to
24:881:66428:17:18:72:540:400:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:declines in value-added relative to gross trade among adopting partners, such that the spread
24:882:66521:18:18:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of RTAs over time can account for 15% of the decline in the world value-added to export
24:883:66608:18:18:72:540:364:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratio  over  time.   In  contrast,  other  major  structural  changes  in  the  global  economy  (e.g.,
24:884:66711:18:18:72:540:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the increasing weight of emerging markets in global GDP) play a minimal role.  Changes in
24:885:66800:18:18:72:540:328:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sector-level patterns of input use and demand are also relatively unimportant, as compared
24:886:66890:18:18:72:218:310:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to the role of trade frictions.
24:887:66921:18:18:89:539:292:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Our  results  have  a  number  of  implications  for  future  research.   First,  the  value-added
24:888:67019:18:18:72:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:data  we  provide  are  immediately  useful  for  parameterizing  quantitative  models.   Because
24:889:67116:18:18:72:540:256:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the value-added content is falling over time, shifting from gross to value-added export data
24:890:67208:18:18:72:540:238:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in empirical applications is more important now than ever.  Second, the large role of trade
24:891:67299:18:18:72:540:220:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions in explaining changes in gross versus value-added trade calls for re-visiting classic
24:892:67394:18:18:72:540:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:questions about the burden of trade frictions.  For example, how do gross trade frictions map
24:893:67487:18:18:72:540:184:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:into reduced form frictions for trading value added, or how do RTAs induce trade creation
24:894:67576:18:17:72:540:166:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:vs. diversion in value-added exports?  Third, if one knows the factor/task contents employed
24:895:67668:17:18:72:540:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in producing value added, one can immediately convert value-added exports into bilateral
24:896:67756:18:18:72:540:131:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:measures of factor/task trade.  Therefore, the type of value-added data we provide should
24:897:67845:18:18:72:540:113:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:be useful in analyzing models of factor/task trade, including the role of factor/task trade in
24:898:67939:18:0:72:322:95:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:explaining changes in the distribution of income.

35:1262:82157:7:0:190:201:140:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:year
35:1263:82161:0:8:124:188:284:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Final Non-Manufacturing
35:1264:82184:8:8:124:188:276:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Input Non-Manufacturing
35:1265:82207:8:9:124:175:268:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Final Manufacturing
35:1266:82226:9:8:124:175:259:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Input Manufacturing
35:1267:82245:8:134:124:163:251:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Trade Frictions
35:1268:82260:134:0:136:238:117:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:?:(a) Non-Manufacturing
35:1269:82300:0:7:325:533:147:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:19701980199020002010
35:1270:82320:7:0:424:435:140:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:year
35:1271:82324:0:8:358:422:192:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Final Non-Manufacturing
35:1272:82347:8:8:358:422:184:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Input Non-Manufacturing
35:1273:82370:8:8:358:409:176:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Final Manufacturing
35:1274:82389:8:8:358:409:168:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Input Manufacturing
35:1275:82408:8:43:358:397:160:0:g_d0_f31:0:g_d0_f31:?:Trade Frictions
35:1276:82423:43:0:380:461:117:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:?:(b) Manufacturing
36:1277:82442:0:27:72:236:708:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:A    Data Appendix
36:1278:82460:27:14:72:540:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This appendix describes the data sources and procedures that we use to construct the input-
36:1279:82551:14:35:72:466:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:output framework.  Further details are available from the authors on request.
36:1280:82628:35:22:72:283:632:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:A.1    National Accounts Data
36:1281:82657:22:14:72:540:610:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To measure macroeconomic aggregates and sector-level production over time, we primarily
36:1282:82744:14:15:72:539:596:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f33:B:rely on the United Nations National Accounts Main Aggregates Database [http://unstats.
36:1283:82830:15:14:72:539:581:12:g_d0_f33:12:g_d0_f2:B:un.org/unsd/snaama].  For all countries other than China, we take aggregate GDP and the
36:1284:82917:14:15:72:540:567:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:expenditure side breakdown of GDP (consumption, investment, government spending, ex-
36:1285:83001:15:14:72:540:552:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ports, and imports) from the UN data.  We use data from the World Development Indicators
36:1286:83089:14:15:72:540:538:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:[http://databank.worldbank.org] for China.33We also take sector-level GDP data from
36:1287:83172:15:14:72:540:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these sources for the four composite sectors, and include the sector definitions in Table A2.
36:1288:83265:14:15:72:540:509:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Finally, we extract goods and services trade shares in total exports and total imports from
36:1289:83356:15:14:72:540:494:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the IMF Balance of Payments Statistics (BPM5) [http://elibrary-data.imf.org/], and
36:1290:83438:14:14:72:540:480:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we use these shares to split exports and imports from the expenditure-side GDP data into
36:1291:83526:14:35:72:170:466:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:goods and services.
36:1292:83545:35:22:72:193:431:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:A.2    Trade Data
36:1293:83562:22:15:72:540:409:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We combine bilateral commodity trade statistics with multilateral totals from the national
36:1294:83652:15:14:72:540:394:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:accounts  to  generate  a  bilateral  trade  database  that  is  consistent  in  level  terms  with  the
36:1295:83756:14:15:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:national accounts.  First, we discuss bilateral commodity trade data sources, classification of
36:1296:83851:15:14:72:540:365:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade according to final and intermediate use, and correspondences to industry data.  Second,
36:1297:83944:14:15:72:540:351:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we discuss how we harmonize the commodity trade data with national accounts data.  Third,
36:1298:84033:15:14:72:409:336:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we discuss how we deal with missing bilateral services trade data.
36:1299:84099:14:14:89:539:322:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f33:A:For  bilateral  goods  trade,  we  draw  on  the  NBER-UN  Database  [http://cid.econ.
36:1300:84185:14:15:71:540:308:12:g_d0_f33:12:g_d0_f2:B:ucdavis.edu]  for  1970-2000  and  the  CEPII  BACI  Database  [http://www.cepii.fr]  for
36:1301:84274:15:14:71:539:293:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1995-2009.   This  data  is  reported  on  a  commodity-basis.   We  assign  commodities  to  end
36:1302:84371:14:15:71:540:279:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f33:B:uses  and  industries  using  existing  correspondences  from  the  World  Bank  [http://wits.
36:1303:84465:15:14:71:539:264:12:g_d0_f33:12:g_d0_f2:B:worldbank.org].  To assign commodities to end uses, we use correspondences between SITC
36:1304:84552:14:15:71:539:250:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(Revision 2) 4-digit or HS (1996 Revision) 6-digit commodities and the BEC end use clas-
36:1305:84640:15:14:71:539:235:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sifications.  To assign commodities to industries, we use correspondences between SITC and
36:1306:84730:14:15:71:539:221:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:HS categories and ISIC (Revision 2) industries.34We then map ISIC sectors into our four
36:1307:84817:15:14:71:164:206:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:composite sectors.
36:1308:84835:14:14:89:539:192:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In  general,  these  correspondences  provide  a  clean  mapping  from  the  trade  to  end  use
36:1309:84931:14:15:71:539:178:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:or industry classifications.  We are able to match upwards of 95% of trade to industries in
36:1310:85022:15:14:71:539:163:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:most country years, and the match quality for the post-1995 BACI data is nearly perfect.
36:1311:85110:14:18:71:369:149:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:However, there are a few complications that we note here.
36:1312:85167:18:16:81:539:131:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:33Comparing the WDI to the UN data, values for most countries are nearly identical. China is an exception.
36:1313:85273:16:8:72:540:115:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:Upon examination of other sources (e.g., Chinese national accounts), the WDI data appears more accurate.
36:1314:85377:8:16:81:539:107:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:34For  a  few  unassigned  commodities,  we  use  a  supplemental  correspondence  between  SITC  and  ISIC
36:1315:85484:16:0:72:524:91:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:classifications developed by Marc Muendler [http://econ.ucsd.edu/muendler/html/resource.html].
37:1316:85580:0:15:89:539:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:First,  though  most  commodities  map  into  unique  end  use  classifications,  some  do  not
37:1317:85675:15:14:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(e.g., “motor spirits” in the BEC classification is a mixed use commodity).  For these mixed
37:1318:85767:14:15:72:497:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:use commodities, we assign half the value to final use and half to intermediate use.
37:1319:85851:15:14:89:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second, some trade cannot be assigned using the correspondences.  This problem occurs
37:1320:85936:14:15:72:540:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:primarily in the NBER-UN data, where there are aggregate categories ending in ’X’ (e.g.,
37:1321:86024:15:14:72:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:04XX)  for  some  countries  and  years  that  include  trade  that  could  not  be  disaggregated.
37:1322:86123:14:15:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Where possible, we map directly from higher levels of aggregation (e.g., SITC 2 digit codes)
37:1323:86215:15:14:72:540:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to sectors/uses.  Where necessary,  we split unallocated SITC 1-digit residual trade across
37:1324:86306:14:14:72:437:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sectors/uses using the world-level allocation shares for these categories.
37:1325:86380:14:15:89:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Having formed a complete bilateral goods trade dataset, we need to harmonize the levels
37:1326:86467:15:14:72:540:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of bilateral trade with aggregate exports and imports in the national accounts. The procedure
37:1327:86560:14:15:72:540:549:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we  use  is  as  follows.   First,  we  harmonize  levels  for  aggregate  bilateral  goods  trade  with
37:1328:86664:15:14:72:540:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:national accounts totals.35Second, we use the share of each sector and end use in bilateral
37:1329:86755:14:15:72:540:520:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade from the commodity trade data to allocate the harmonized bilateral goods trade to
37:1330:86842:15:14:72:540:505:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sectors and end uses.  This yields a sector-level bilateral final and intermediate goods trade
37:1331:86936:14:15:72:358:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dataset that aggregates to match the national accounts.
37:1332:86991:15:14:89:539:476:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Next, we turn to bilateral services trade.  Unfortunately, bilateral services trade data has
37:1333:87083:14:14:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:not  been  collected  with  the  same  scope  and  rigor  as  goods  trade  data;  Data  on  bilateral
37:1334:87185:14:15:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:services trade is incomplete today, and virtually nonexistent going back in time.  Therefore
37:1335:87277:15:14:72:540:433:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:we impute these flows using multilateral data on services trade along with bilateral trade
37:1336:87367:14:15:72:540:419:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shares  for  goods.   This  procedure  is  sensible,  given  the  strong  correlation  between  goods
37:1337:87468:15:14:72:540:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and services trade shares in modern data.  We run a constrained least squares program that
37:1338:87558:14:15:72:540:390:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:finds the bilateral services flows that minimize deviations between imputed services trade
37:1339:87648:15:14:72:540:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:shares and target shares (based on goods trade), subject to the constraint that the sum of
37:1340:87738:14:35:72:493:361:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:the bilateral flows be equal to multilateral exports and imports for each country.36
37:1341:87822:35:22:72:259:326:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:A.3    Input-Output Tables
37:1342:87848:22:14:72:540:304:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:We  obtain  input-output  tables  from  the  OECD  Input-Output  Database  (1995  and  2011
37:1343:87939:14:15:72:539:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:editions)  [http://www.oecd.org/sti/inputoutput/]  and  the  IDE-JETRO  Asian  Input-
37:1344:88024:15:14:72:539:275:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Output Tables [http://www.ide.go.jp/].  Benchmark tables are available for countries and
37:1345:88112:14:18:71:539:261:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:years listed in Table A1.  The mapping between sectors in the input-output data and our
37:1346:88199:18:16:81:539:243:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:35In this step, we take bilateral trade shares from the commodity trade data and multiply them with the
37:1347:88302:16:12:72:540:227:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:level of goods exports and imports reported in the national accounts.  This procedure yields two conflicting
37:1348:88410:12:12:72:540:215:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:estimates for each bilateral trade flow, one ‘exporter report’ from multiplying the trade share times reported
37:1349:88520:12:12:72:540:203:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:multilateral exports in a given source country and a corresponding ‘importer report’ from multiplying the
37:1350:88625:12:12:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:trade share times reported multilateral imports in a given destination.  To reconcile the flows, we average the
37:1351:88736:12:12:72:540:179:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:flows to form a single bilateral flow and then add or subtract the residual (e.g., the exporter report minus
37:1352:88844:12:11:72:540:167:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the reconciled trade flow) from trade flows with the rest of the world.  This procedure occasionally results
37:1353:88952:11:12:72:540:156:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:in negative values for trade with the rest of the world, concentrated in particular countries and years where
37:1354:89061:12:12:72:540:144:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:either trade or national accounts data are suspect (e.g., for Argentina in the early 1970’s).  We adjust the
37:1355:89169:12:12:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:data to eliminate these zeros using a constrained least squares procedure that penalizes changes in trade
37:1356:89274:12:9:72:479:120:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:shares among the 42 countries in our sample.  The necessarily adjustments are trivially small.
37:1357:89368:9:15:81:539:111:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:36The  target  shares  are  constructed  by  multiplying  goods  trade  shares  with  multilateral  services  trade
37:1358:89483:15:12:72:540:96:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:from the national accounts.  As in the goods data, this yields conflicting exporter and importer reports, and
37:1359:89592:12:0:72:430:84:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:we set the reconciled flow equal to the average report, as in the previous footnote.
38:1360:89678:0:15:72:327:708:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:four composite sectors are included in Table A2.37
38:1361:89728:15:14:89:539:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In  the  input-output  tables,  there  are  accounting  variables  that  we  did  not  explicitly
38:1362:89825:14:15:71:539:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:define in our framework, such as statistical discrepancies, other adjustments, non-comparable
38:1363:89918:15:14:71:539:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:imports, and net taxes.38In general, these tend to be small or exactly zero (due to how data
38:1364:90010:14:15:71:539:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is reported from national authorities to the OECD). We drop net taxes, and therefore define
38:1365:90101:15:14:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value  added  as  output  (the  row  sum  of  the  tables)  less  inputs  used  (the  column  sum  of
38:1366:90202:14:15:71:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the domestic and import input-output tables).39We allocate other adjustments and non-
38:1367:90287:15:14:71:539:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:comparable exports and imports to the services sector, based on the fact that non-comparable
38:1368:90379:14:14:71:539:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:imports in the U.S. input-output accounts are entirely services.  Finally, values in the input-
38:1369:90474:14:15:71:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:output tables are reported in national currency in the OECD input-output tables. We convert
38:1370:90565:15:14:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these to U.S. dollars using end-of-year exchange rates from the IMF’s International Financial
38:1371:90658:14:15:71:539:549:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Statistics (AE series) and OECDStat.  Values in the IDE-JETRO tables are reported in U.S.
38:1372:90747:15:14:71:108:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dollars.
38:1373:90755:14:15:89:539:520:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Because the input-output and national accounts data are taken from separate sources,
38:1374:90839:15:14:71:539:505:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:they are not identical.  On top of this,  input-output tables are available only for selected
38:1375:90932:14:15:71:539:491:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:benchmark years, which differ across countries.  To reconcile data sources and extrapolate
38:1376:91022:15:14:71:539:476:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from benchmark to non-benchmark years,  we adjust the benchmark input-output data to
38:1377:91106:14:14:71:539:462:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:match the time-varying national accounts and trade data using a constrained least squares
38:1378:91195:14:15:71:539:448:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:procedure.  In doing so, we give priority to matching the national accounts and trade data,
38:1379:91286:15:14:71:539:433:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:both because they are available in all years and measured consistently through time (likely
38:1380:91377:14:15:71:539:419:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with less error).  The objective is to minimize the weighted squared distance between the
38:1381:91466:15:14:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:adjusted data and input-output data for benchmark years (or interpolations thereof), subject
38:1382:91558:14:15:71:457:390:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:to a set of accounting identities.  We pause here to spell out this procedure.
38:1383:91636:15:14:89:540:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:To define notation, letIDiandIIibe 4×4 matrices of domestic and imported inputs, with
38:1384:91721:14:15:71:539:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:elementsIDi(s,s′) andIIi(s,s′), letfDiandfIibe 4×1 vectors of domestic and imported
38:1385:91804:15:14:71:539:346:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:final demand, and letyibe a 4×1 vector of sector-level output.  Further, the 4×1 vector
38:1386:91891:14:14:71:428:332:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of exports isxi.  Note we suppress year notation here for convenience.
38:1387:91961:14:15:89:539:318:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In the national accounts and trade data, we observe the following objects, denoted with
38:1388:92048:15:14:71:539:303:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:bars to indicate data.  We observe sector-level value added in a 1×4 vector   ̄vaiand (scaler)
38:1389:92142:14:15:71:539:289:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:aggregate final demand ̄fi.  We also observe sector-level exports  ̄xi.  On the import side, we
38:1390:92237:15:14:71:540:274:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:observe  imports  by  sector  and  end  use  for  goods  – ̄fIi(s)  and ̄IIi(s)  fors=  1,2,3  –  and
38:1391:92338:14:15:71:192:260:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:services imports   ̄mit(4).
38:1392:92365:15:14:89:540:245:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Sector-level  input  use  for  domestic  and  imported  intermediates  (IDi,IIi),  sector-level
38:1393:92460:14:15:71:539:231:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:domestic  and  imported  final  goods  absorption  (fDi,fIi(4)),  and  sector-level  gross  output
38:1394:92558:15:14:71:539:216:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(yi(s))  are  treated  as  unknowns.   We  observe  ‘target’  values  for  each  of  these  unknowns,
38:1395:92659:14:18:71:539:202:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:based on the value of these variables in the input-output tables for benchmark years, and
38:1396:92748:18:15:81:539:184:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:37The  original  OECD  data  covers  35  sectors  for  years  before  1990  and  48  sectors  for  years  after  1995,
38:1397:92866:15:12:72:540:169:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:though  not  all  countries  report  data  at  this  level  of  disaggregation.   The  IDE-JETRO  tables  contain  24
38:1398:92983:12:9:72:298:157:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:sectors.  Aggregation enables us to link these tables.
38:1399:93037:9:15:81:539:148:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:38Further,  in  the  pre-1995  data,  a  few  countries  have  negative  entries  in  their  import  tables,  due  to
38:1400:93154:15:12:72:540:133:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:treatment  of  transport  margins,  recycled  products,  and  measurement  error.   We  eliminate  these  using  a
38:1401:93268:12:12:72:540:121:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:procedure similar to that which we use to merge the IO tables with the production and trade data below,
38:1402:93371:12:9:72:209:109:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:so omit details here for brevity.
38:1403:93404:9:15:81:539:100:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:39Recall that we adjust this data to match national accounts value added by sector.  The only way value
38:1404:93507:15:12:72:540:85:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:added from the input-output tables is used is to create value-added to output ratios, which are the basis for
38:1405:93616:12:0:72:465:73:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:generating target values for gross output in the harmonization procedure described below.
39:1406:93707:0:15:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:linear  interpolations  between  benchmark  years.40We  construct  target  sector  output  by
39:1407:93800:15:14:71:539:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:dividing value added from the national accounts by the value added to output ratio in the
39:1408:93889:14:15:71:539:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:input-output tables.  We also construct target sector final expenditure by multiplying final
39:1409:93981:15:14:71:458:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:demand in the national accounts by sector shares from input-output tables.
39:1410:94055:14:15:89:539:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In each year, we solve for values for these ‘unknown’ variables that:  (a) minimize differ-
39:1411:94146:15:14:71:539:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ences between the adjusted data and raw data observed in benchmark years (or interpolations
39:1412:94237:14:15:71:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:thereof between benchmark years); and (b) satisfy adding up constraints.  We constrain the
39:1413:94327:15:14:71:539:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:solution to match sector-level GDP, exports and imports of final and intermediate goods for
39:1414:94418:14:14:71:539:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:each of the three goods sectors, exports and imports of services, and aggregate final expen-
39:1415:94510:14:15:71:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:diture.  These constraints provide a lot of discipline in extrapolating the benchmark data.
39:1416:94601:15:14:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:For example, we exactly match the time series for sector-level intermediate goods imports
39:1417:94690:14:15:71:433:549:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:in all countries, which is the key determinant of vertical specialization.
39:1418:94764:15:39:89:352:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:With target values denoted by stars, then we solve:

45:1859:105731:30:22:72:241:269:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:B.5    Additional Details
45:1860:105756:22:14:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In computing trade frictions,  expenditure weights,  and counterfactuals,  we confront a few
45:1861:105848:14:18:72:200:233:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:additional complications.
45:1862:105873:18:12:81:362:215:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:A:42Seehttp://www.rug.nl/research/ggdc/data/pwt/pwt-8.1.
45:1863:105927:12:16:81:539:203:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:43Because the Penn World Tables capital stock series is built using an investment-specific deflator, this
45:1864:106032:16:12:72:540:187:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:real investment series allows for investment-specific technical change, which is embedded in ˆsit.  In making
45:1865:106141:12:12:72:540:175:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:the assumption that ˆsitis exogenous, we are making two underlying assumptions:  both the nominal share
45:1866:106244:12:12:72:540:163:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:of  investment  in  final  expenditure  and  the  rate  of  investment-specific  technical  are  exogenous.   This  is
45:1867:106362:12:12:72:540:151:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:substantively the same as in Levchenko and Zhang (2016).  While Levchenko and Zhang take the (nominal)
45:1868:106464:12:12:72:540:139:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:savings  rate  as  exogenous,  this  is  equivalent  to  taking  the  nominal  investment  share  as  given  since  their
45:1869:106585:12:12:72:540:127:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:baseline  model  has  balanced  trade.   They  also  use  the  Penn  World  Tables  capital  stock  to  compute  real
45:1870:106702:12:0:72:165:115:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:investment, as we do.
46:1871:106725:0:15:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:Rest of the WorldAs noted in Section 1.2, we possess input-output data for 42 individual
46:1872:106813:15:14:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:countries  and  collect  remaining  countries  in  a  composite  rest-of-the-world  region.   In  the
46:1873:106914:14:15:72:540:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:model accounting exercise, we focus on the 37 countries that have complete time series data
46:1874:107005:15:14:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:from 1970-2009, and add the remaining 5 Eastern European countries to the rest-of-the-world
46:1875:107096:14:15:72:540:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:composite.  Because we do not have input-output and price data for this rest-of-the-world
46:1876:107185:15:14:72:363:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:composite, we need to make two additional assumptions.
46:1877:107239:14:15:89:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:First, we assume that changes in gross exports from each country to the rest of the world
46:1878:107328:15:14:72:539:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(̂EXi,ROW,t(s)) are exogenous.  This means that the gross output market clearing condition

46:1892:107729:14:15:72:341:460:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Equation (B32) in the numerical solution procedure.
46:1893:107780:15:14:89:539:445:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Second,  we  assume  that  gross  output  prices  for  imports  from  the  rest  of  the  world

46:1895:107969:15:14:72:540:416:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade frictions{ˆτXROW,it(s,s′),ˆτFROW,it(s)}measure both changes in frictions and unobserved
46:1896:108062:14:15:72:143:402:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:price changes.
46:1897:108076:15:14:89:540:387:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f14:A:In all simulations, we feed realized values for̂EXi,ROW,t(s) and{ˆτXROW,it(s,s′),ˆτFROW,it(s)}
46:1898:108170:14:15:72:540:373:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:into the model.  Therefore, these nuisance parameters do not play a significant role in ex-
46:1899:108261:15:31:72:486:358:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:plaining differences in simulated data across alternative counterfactual scenarios.
46:1900:108344:31:14:72:539:327:12:g_d0_f8:12:g_d0_f2:B:ZerosIn  the  input-output  and  trade  data,  there  are  a  small  number  of  zero  entries  (or
46:1901:108443:14:15:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:near zeros), almost exclusively in the bilateral trade data and mostly concentrated in first
46:1902:108535:15:14:72:540:298:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:half of the sample period.  These create technical problems in computing trade frictions and
46:1903:108627:14:15:72:539:284:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:solving the model.  If a particular value is zero in the initial period,  then  ˆxtis undefined.
46:1904:108723:15:14:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Alternatively, if a particular value is positive in the initial period and then zero in period
46:1905:108817:14:14:72:540:255:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:B:t+ 1, CES preferences imply that trade frictions fall from infinity to a finite value.  Since
46:1906:108910:14:15:72:540:241:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these occurrences are rare, we deal with them by adopting an approximation.  We take any
46:1907:108998:15:14:72:540:226:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value  in  the  bilateral  final  and  and  intermediate  goods  shipments  data  that  is  less  than
46:1908:109100:14:15:72:540:212:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:$1000 and set it equal to $1000, and then recompute other objects in the data (e.g., gross
46:1909:109190:15:14:72:540:197:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:output) as necessary to satisfy adding up conditions.  This approximation plays no role in
46:1910:109280:14:0:72:220:183:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:understanding model results.
47:1911:109310:0:27:72:350:708:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:C    Model Accounting Appendix
47:1912:109340:27:14:72:540:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This appendix documents how trade frictions and expenditure weights change over time.  It
47:1913:109429:14:15:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:also describes how we estimate the response of trade frictions to adoption of regional trade
47:1914:109521:15:34:72:490:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:agreements and construct the counterfactual trade cost series used in Section 4.2.
47:1915:109603:34:22:72:303:618:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:C.1    Changes in Trade Frictions
47:1916:109636:22:15:72:540:596:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:In  our  framework,  changes  in  trade  frictions  vary  across  bilateral  pairs  at  the  sector  (or
47:1917:109740:15:14:72:540:581:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sector-pair) level.  To summarize them, we aggregate the frictions in to composite indexes.
47:1918:109831:14:15:72:540:567:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:These indexes can be constructed for any time horizon.  In our analysis below, we focus on
47:1919:109921:15:14:72:539:552:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:long horizon changes in trade frictions, from 1970 to 2009.44Therefore, the hat-notation in
47:1920:110012:14:15:72:540:538:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this section can be interpreted as denoting the ratio of frictions in 2009 relative to 1970, and
47:1921:110108:15:14:72:274:523:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade weights are based on 1970 values.

48:2027:111734:0:15:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:estimate that the ad valorem tariff equivalent of iceberg trade frictions declined by 26% from
48:2028:111828:15:14:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:1970 to 2000 for 12 industrial countries.46For reasonable values of iceberg costs in 1970,
48:2029:111918:14:15:72:540:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:this translates into a decline in iceberg frictions on the order of 17%.47For comparison, our
48:2030:112011:15:14:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:estimates imply that iceberg frictions fell by 23% over the 1970-2000 interval, close to the
48:2031:112103:14:15:72:539:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Jacks et al.  estimate.48Comparing this to our estimate over the entire 1970-2008 interval,
48:2032:112194:15:14:72:400:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:iceberg trade costs continued to fall (rapidly) during the 2000’s.
48:2033:112260:14:15:89:539:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Disaggregating this global result, we present disaggregated trade friction indexes by coun-
48:2034:112351:15:14:72:540:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:try and sector in Table C1.  There is significant dispersion in estimated trade cost declines
48:2035:112444:14:14:72:540:592:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:across countries.  The summary statistic  ˆω(−i)iis included in the last column of Table C1.
48:2036:112536:14:15:71:539:578:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:The quartiles of the distribution are{0.51,0.64,0.79}.  An important feature of these changes
48:2037:112629:15:14:72:540:563:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:is that they line up with changes in openness in a sensible way.  To illustrate this, Figure C1
48:2038:112724:14:15:72:540:549:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:plots the log ratio of trade frictions against the log ratio of openness.  Turning to the sector
48:2039:112820:15:15:72:539:534:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:level indexes ˆωF(−i)i(s) and ˆωX−(i)i(s′,s) in the body of the table, trade frictions decline in 184
48:2040:112921:15:15:71:539:519:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:out of 222 (83%) of cases.  Looking at the medians in the bottom row, declines are somewhat
48:2041:113012:15:14:71:539:504:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:larger for manufactures (sector 2) than for non-manufactures (sector 1), particularly for final
48:2042:113107:14:35:71:104:490:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:goods.
48:2043:113113:35:22:71:344:455:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:C.2    Changes in Expenditure Weights
48:2044:113150:22:14:71:539:433:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Table C2 presents changes in input and final goods expenditure weights over time.  These
48:2045:113238:14:15:71:410:419:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:are the cumulated values of ˆωFi(s) and ˆωXi(s′,s) from 1970-2008.49
48:2046:113306:15:14:89:539:404:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Three general points are worth making.  First,  the changes in final goods expenditure
48:2047:113392:14:15:72:540:390:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:weights  are  broadly  similar  across  sectors.   This  holds  despite  the  fact  that  the  share  of
48:2048:113496:15:14:72:540:375:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:services in final expenditure is rising.  The reason is that this rise in the services share is
48:2049:113591:14:18:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:largely accounted for in the model by the complementarity between manufactures and non-
48:2050:113678:18:16:81:539:343:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:46In the main body of the paper, Jacks et al.  use country-level GDP data to measure production (and
48:2051:113778:16:12:72:540:327:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:hence domestic trade).  In Appendix B of their paper, they use data on gross output for goods producing
48:2052:113881:12:12:72:540:315:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:sectors instead.  Our approach to measuring frictions is closer to this second gross output method, and so
48:2053:113987:12:8:72:194:303:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:we use it as our benchmark.
48:2054:114014:8:11:81:540:295:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:47Letτ= (1 +t) be the iceberg friction and lettis the ad valorem tariff equivalent.  Jacks et al.  report
48:2055:114119:11:8:73:90:284:7:g_d0_f18:5:g_d0_f34:F:t2000
48:2056:114124:8:0:73:503:276:7:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f6:F:t1970= 0.74.  To compare this to our figure, we need to convert this into a ratio of iceberg frictions:
48:2057:114227:0:8:508:535:284:7:g_d0_f5:5:g_d0_f34:?:1+t2000
48:2058:114234:8:8:508:540:276:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:1+t1970.
48:2059:114242:8:12:72:540:268:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:This requires taking a stand on the level of trade frictions in 1970.  A reasonable guess is that trade frictions
48:2060:114355:12:12:72:540:256:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:were 200% in 1970,  since Anderson and van Wincoop (2004) report ad valorem trade costs totaled 170%
48:2061:114455:12:12:72:540:244:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:based on evidence from the 1990’s and early 2000’s.  Given this prior, then the Jacks et al.  tariff equivalent
48:2062:114566:12:9:72:465:232:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:estimates implies that the ratio of iceberg frictions in 2000 relative to 1970 would be 0.83.
48:2063:114659:9:15:81:539:223:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:48There are many technical reasons why the estimates should not match exactly, and it is not the purpose
48:2064:114763:15:12:72:540:208:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:of this paper to conduct a fine-grained comparison of them.  Nonetheless, it is useful to highlight a few basic
48:2065:114874:12:12:72:540:196:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:issues.  First, we use a lower trade elasticity than do Jacks et al.  (4 versus 8).  Second, our sample includes
48:2066:114986:12:12:72:540:184:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:emerging markets, where declines in frictions have been larger.  Both these tend to raise our estimates relative
48:2067:115098:12:12:72:540:172:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:to Jacks et al.  On the other hand, we include services trade, where the Jacks et al.  estimate covers goods
48:2068:115206:12:12:72:540:160:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:trade only.  This tends to attenuate measured changes in aggregate trade frictions, since frictions in services
48:2069:115317:12:12:72:540:148:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:have fallen more slowly than in manufactures (see Table C1).  All together, we conclude that our estimates
48:2070:115423:12:8:72:404:136:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:are in the ballpark of what is currently known about changes in trade costs.
48:2071:115499:8:16:81:539:128:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:49Here  we  exclude  data  for  2009,  during  the  Great  Recession  and  Trade  Collapse.   The  reason  is  that
48:2072:115614:16:12:72:540:112:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:there are sharp changes in demand composition during the recession, which are picked up in the measured
48:2073:115717:12:12:72:540:100:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:expenditure weights.  For example, the final expenditure weight on manufacturing is substantially lower in
48:2074:115823:12:12:72:540:88:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:2009,  reflecting the collapse in demand for manufactures (despite minimal price changes) observed in the
48:2075:115928:12:0:72:475:76:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:data.  Discarding these recession-induced changes gives us a clearer picture of secular trends.
49:2076:116025:0:15:72:540:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:manufactures  in  preferences.   Second,  on  the  input  side,  increases  in  expenditure  weights
49:2077:116125:15:14:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:for  the  use  of  non-manufactures  as  inputs  have  tended  to  pull  input  expenditure  toward
49:2078:116224:14:15:72:540:679:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:non-manufactures over time.  This reflects structural change in IO tables, not accounted for
49:2079:116316:15:14:72:540:664:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by relative price changes.  In contrast, manufactures have become more intensively used in
49:2080:116406:14:15:72:540:650:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:services (indicated in the 2,1 column) for reasons not accounted for by relative prices.  Third,
49:2081:116502:15:14:72:540:635:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:at the individual and country and sector level, there is a good bit of heterogeneity in how
49:2082:116593:14:15:72:540:621:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these expenditure weights evolve.  We implicitly assess whether these structural shifts and
49:2083:116684:15:34:72:303:606:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:heterogeneity matter via the counterfactuals.
49:2084:116729:34:22:72:360:572:14:g_d0_f8:14:g_d0_f8:C:C.3    Trade Frictions and RTA Adoption
49:2085:116768:22:15:72:540:550:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To estimate how trade frictions change with RTA adoption, we model the trade frictions in
49:2086:116857:15:23:72:117:535:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:levels as:
49:2087:116867:23:19:184:426:512:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f9:?:ωXijt(s,s′) = ΦXij(s,s′)ΦXit(s,s′)ΦXjt(s,s′)cXijt(s,s′),

49:2105:117979:14:15:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:frictions  are  differenced  away.   Further,  we  can  control  for  the  time-varying  source  and
49:2106:118079:15:14:72:529:210:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:destination components of trade costs with source-year and destination-year fixed effects.
49:2107:118169:14:15:89:539:196:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Following the discussion in Section 2.2, we estimate these regressions using several alter-

55:2324:126097:0:27:72:379:708:17:g_d0_f8:17:g_d0_f8:C:D    Supplemental Results Appendix
55:2325:126131:27:14:72:540:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:This appendix includes supplemental data and decomposition results, which we omitted from
55:2326:126220:14:15:72:246:667:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the main set of figures for brevity.
55:2327:126256:15:14:89:539:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Table D1 includes data by sector for the world as a whole, along with the share of each
55:2328:126343:14:15:72:540:638:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sector in world trade.  These are the data plotted in Figures 1 and 2.  Table D2 includes data
55:2329:126437:15:14:72:540:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:by  country,  in  the  aggregate  and  by  composite  sector.   These  are  the  country-level  ratios
55:2330:126539:14:14:72:172:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:plotted in Figure 3.
55:2331:126559:14:15:89:539:595:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Section 4, we mentioned that trade composition plays a large role in explaining differ-
55:2332:126649:15:14:72:540:580:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ential changes in value-added to export ratios across countries.  We document that assertion
55:2333:126741:14:15:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:here via a Between-Within decomposition.  LettingV AXitandV AXit(s) denote aggregate
55:2334:126825:15:14:72:540:551:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:and sector-level value-added to export ratios for countryi, then we construct the decompo-
55:2335:126915:14:27:72:156:537:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:sition as follows:
55:2336:126933:27:0:154:211:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:∆V AXit=
55:2337:126941:0:25:214:231:522:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:∑
55:2338:126942:25:0:221:225:497:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:s
55:2339:126943:0:19:234:321:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?: ̄ωit(s)∆V AXit(s)
55:2340:126961:19:10:214:320:491:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:︸︷︷︸
55:2341:126965:10:0:253:282:481:8:g_d0_f3:6:g_d0_f15:?:Withini
55:2342:126972:0:0:323:344:510:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:?:+ +
55:2343:126975:0:25:346:363:522:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:∑
55:2344:126976:25:0:353:357:497:8:g_d0_f10:8:g_d0_f10:?:s
55:2345:126977:0:19:365:454:510:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:?:V AXit(s)∆ωit(s)
55:2346:126993:19:10:346:453:491:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:?:︸︷︷︸
55:2347:126997:10:0:383:417:481:8:g_d0_f3:6:g_d0_f15:A:Betweeni
55:2348:127005:0:58:453:540:510:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f2:A:,(D1)
55:2349:127010:58:0:72:282:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:where  ̄ωit(s)≡ωit(s)+ωi,t−1(s)2withωit(s) =
55:2350:127054:0:8:293:302:465:8:g_d0_f21:8:g_d0_f21:?:∑
55:2351:127055:8:12:302:295:457:6:g_d0_f15:8:g_d0_f21:?:j6=ixijt(s)∑
55:2352:127067:12:0:295:307:445:6:g_d0_f15:6:g_d0_f15:?:j6=i
55:2353:127071:0:9:309:318:454:8:g_d0_f21:8:g_d0_f21:A:∑
55:2354:127072:9:0:317:347:445:6:g_d0_f15:8:g_d0_f3:A:sxijt(s)
55:2355:127080:0:17:349:539:452:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:, andV AXit(s)≡V AXit(s)+V AXi,t−1(s)2.
55:2356:127119:17:15:71:539:435:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:To reiterate, the between term is driven by changes in trade shares for a given country, while
55:2357:127213:15:14:71:539:420:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the within term is driven by changes in value-added to export ratios within sectors in that
55:2358:127304:14:15:71:112:406:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:country.
55:2359:127312:15:14:89:539:391:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:Results for this decomposition are presented in the last two columns of Table D2.  We
55:2360:127397:14:14:71:539:377:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:also  include  data  on  changes  in  the  share  of  manufacturing  of  exports  in  the  table.   The
55:2361:127500:14:15:71:539:363:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:takeaway is that is that changes in the composition of trade are important determinants of
55:2362:127590:15:14:71:539:348:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:difference in the magnitude of value-added to export ratio changes across countries.  Broadly,
55:2363:127684:14:15:71:539:334:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the share of manufactures in trade increases in non-commodity exporter emerging markets.
55:2364:127772:15:14:71:539:319:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:Since the value-added to export ratio is lower for manufacturing than non-manufacturing,
55:2365:127860:14:15:71:539:305:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:an increase in the share of manufacturing in trade mechanically lowers the aggregate VAX
55:2366:127948:15:14:71:539:290:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:ratio.  Interesting, the within term is actually positive in many countries, where increases in
55:2367:128043:14:15:71:539:276:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:the value-added to export ratio in non-manufacturing dominate declines in manufacturing
55:2368:128130:15:14:71:539:261:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:(this  can  occur  either  due  to  the  magnitude  of  the  VAX  changes  themselves,  or  because
55:2369:128229:14:14:71:327:247:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:non-manufacturing has a large weight in exports).
55:2370:128278:14:15:89:539:233:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Figure D1, we project the components of this Between-Within decomposition on in-
55:2371:128361:15:14:71:539:218:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:come  growth,  similar  to  Panel  (b)  of  Figure  3.   The  within  term  tends  to  be  positively
55:2372:128462:14:15:71:539:204:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlated  with  income  growth,  while  the  between  term  tends  to  be  negatively  correlated
55:2373:128561:15:14:71:539:189:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:with income growth.  As such,  the fact that growth predicts declines in the overall value-
55:2374:128652:14:15:71:539:175:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:added to export ratio is entirely due to the between term.  That is, fast growing countries
55:2375:128743:15:14:71:539:160:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:tend  to  see  the  share  of  manufacturing  in  their  exports  rise,  and  this  drives  the  overall
55:2376:128847:14:15:71:451:146:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation between declining VAX ratios and growth at the country level.
55:2377:128920:15:14:89:539:131:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Section 2.2, we discussed how bilateral value-added to gross export ratios respond to
55:2378:129008:14:15:71:539:117:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:distance,  and how this response has changed over time.  Here we document this result in
55:2379:129096:15:14:71:539:102:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:a complementary way, which allows us to discuss the role of additional gravity proxies for
55:2380:129186:14:0:71:539:88:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:trade costs as well.  Building on Equation (3), we estimate a gravity-type equation in long
56:2381:129279:0:27:72:128:708:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:differences:
56:2382:129291:27:29:132:540:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:∆ log(yijt) = ∆φyit+ ∆φyjt+ ̆βytlog(distij) +  ̆γytGravProxyij+ ∆ηyijt,(D2)
56:2383:129366:29:14:72:540:652:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:wheret={1975,2005}and ̆βyt≡∆ ̃βytis an estimate of the change in regression coefficients
56:2384:129454:14:15:72:539:638:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:over time.51While log(distij) is bilateral distance (as in the main text),GravProxyijis a
56:2385:129543:15:14:71:539:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:stand-in variable for conventional gravity proxies included in the regression, with associated
56:2386:129637:14:15:71:540:609:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:change in the gravity coefficient given by  ̆γyt.  We examine three commonly used trade cost
56:2387:129729:15:14:71:539:594:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:proxies to the regression – common language,  common borders (contiguity),  and common
56:2388:129815:14:15:71:155:580:12:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f3:B:colonial origin.52
56:2389:129833:15:14:89:539:565:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In Table D3, the estimates in Column (1) of Panel A indicate that declines in the ratio
56:2390:129920:14:14:71:539:551:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of  value-added  to  gross  exports  are  smaller  for  countries  that  are  farther  apart.   Looking
56:2391:130023:14:15:71:539:537:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:at gross and value-added exports separately, changes in both gross and value-added trade
56:2392:130111:15:14:71:539:522:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:are smaller for countries that are far apart, but distance increasingly dampens gross exports
56:2393:130204:14:15:71:509:508:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:more than value-added exports.  These results confirm the results plotted in Figure 4.
56:2394:130290:15:14:89:539:493:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:A:In contrast to distance, we see that gross and value-added exports respond similarly to
56:2395:130377:14:15:71:539:479:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:other  gravity  proxies  for  trade  costs  in  columns  (2)-(5)  of  Table  D3.   Common  borders,
56:2396:130476:15:14:71:539:464:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:common language and colonial origin are associated with smaller changes in both gross and
56:2397:130565:14:15:71:539:450:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:value-added trade, with roughly similar magnitudes.  As a result, there is no change in the
56:2398:130656:15:14:71:539:435:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:correlation of value-added to export ratios with these variables over time.  Moreover, adding
56:2399:130749:14:14:71:539:421:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:these  additional  explanatory  variables  does  not  change  the  magnitude  of  the  differential
56:2400:130848:14:15:71:539:407:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:responses of gross and value-added trade to distance.  In sum, distance stands out in terms
56:2401:130939:15:257:71:199:392:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:B:of its explanatory power.
56:2402:130964:257:16:81:539:135:7:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f6:F:51Note that by estimating the gravity equation in first differences, we are implicitly including a pair-specific
56:2403:131076:16:8:72:509:119:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:F:fixed effect that is differenced away.  This controls for all omitted, bilateral, time-invariant variables.
